Lecture 3: Introduction to APIs
Module: LLM Full-Stack Development
Lesson Type: Conceptual Foundation + First Principles Thinking
Learning Outcomes
By the end of this lesson, you'll be able to:
Understand what an API is and why it's necessary from first principles
Identify the limitations of building standalone chat-based applications without APIs
Apply first principles thinking to understand why software components need to communicate
Connect the concept of APIs to real-world use cases like Vikram's Facebook ads analysis tool
Plan and design a chatbot that integrates UI, back-end, and LLM using APIs
Prepare for building complete OPT (Operating Model, Processes, Tasks) automation systems with proper API architecture
3.1 Why Do We Need APIs? A First Principles Approach
In the previous lecture, we successfully built a complete chat-based application using Gradio. This application had three key components:
A front-end UI built with Gradio
A back-end function that processed user input
An LLM connection that provided AI intelligence
The natural question that arises is: If we can already build chat-based applications with LLMs, back-ends, and front-ends, why do we need to learn about APIs?
Key Insight:
APIs are essential when you need different software systems to communicate with each other. While a standalone chat application works for a single user interface, real-world applications require multiple services to work together—user authentication, data storage, external integrations, and more. This is where APIs become the bridge between different software components.
3.2 Understanding APIs Through First Principles Thinking
Throughout this course, we emphasize first principles thinking—understanding the fundamental 'why' behind every technology before diving into the 'how.' This approach helps you build a deeper, more lasting understanding rather than just memorizing definitions.
What is First Principles Thinking?
First principles thinking means breaking down complex problems into their most basic, foundational truths and reasoning up from there. Instead of accepting definitions at face value, we ask 'why' repeatedly until we understand the core reason something exists.
3.3 Real-World Use Case: Vikram's Facebook Ads Analyzer
To make the concept of APIs concrete, let's examine a real project from one of your fellow students, Vikram, who is actively posting on the 0 to 100X challenge.
The Problem Vikram Solved
Vikram built a tool using Comet Browser that analyzes 100+ Facebook ads in under 10 minutes. This is particularly valuable for performance marketing professionals who need to quickly evaluate large volumes of advertising content.
Why This Requires APIs
Vikram's tool requires multiple systems to communicate:
Comet Browser to scrape and collect Facebook ads data
An LLM to analyze the advertising content
A back-end system to process and coordinate the analysis
A UI to display results to the user
Without APIs, these components couldn't communicate with each other. Each piece would exist in isolation, unable to share data or coordinate actions.
3.4 The Limitations of Standalone Applications
When you build a chat-based application using Gradio, all components are tightly integrated into a single application. This works well for simple use cases, but has significant limitations:
Limitation
Why It's a Problem
No Reusability
You can't use the same LLM logic in a mobile app, web app, or other services without duplicating code
No External Integration
Other services or applications can't connect to your chatbot to use its capabilities
Limited Scalability
Difficult to handle multiple users or distribute work across different servers
No Service Separation
Can't update the UI without affecting the back-end logic, or vice versa

 
3.5 What APIs Enable: Communication Between Software Systems
APIs (Application Programming Interfaces) solve these limitations by providing a standardized way for different software systems to communicate. Think of an API as a messenger that takes requests, tells a system what you want, and returns the response.
Core Benefits of APIs
Reusability: Write your back-end logic once, and connect it to multiple front-ends (web, mobile, desktop)
Integration: Allow other services and applications to connect to your system
Separation of Concerns: Update UI without changing back-end logic, or update back-end without breaking the UI
Scalability: Distribute work across multiple servers and handle many users simultaneously
Security: Implement authentication and control who can access your services

HTTP Methods:
 The fundamental "verbs" used in API communication:
GET – retrieve data
POST – create data
PUT / PATCH – update data
DELETE – remove data
API Status Codes:
 Standard responses from servers:
2xx – success
4xx – client error (e.g., 404: Not Found)
5xx – server error
3.6 Building Towards OPT Automation
The ultimate goal of this module is to help you automate tasks in your work using the OPT framework—identifying tasks that can be handled by a single prompt to an AI system. To build effective OPT automation, you need to understand how to create systems where:
The UI collects user input
An API processes that input and sends it to the appropriate back-end service
The LLM performs the actual task
The response flows back through the API to display in the UI
Real-World Success Stories
Several students have already achieved significant results using these principles:
Sha Shanand (VP at SOTI): Automated lead qualification and customer support processes, achieving a 39% productivity increase for his support team
Design Lead at Apple: Automated majority of his work using OPT principles and UI-first development
Fani (BCom graduate with non-technical background): Now works as an AI generalist in a fast-growing startup after completing the cohort
3.7 The Learning Philosophy: Experience First, Then Understanding
This course takes a unique approach to learning: you'll be asked to build things before being taught exactly how they work. This might feel frustrating at times, but it's intentional and effective for several reasons:
You'll experience the actual problems that APIs solve
When you watch the lecture afterward, you'll understand why each concept matters
The context from your struggles will make the solutions more memorable
You'll develop problem-solving skills that transfer to real-world projects

Students who have been most successful with this course share a common practice: they take handwritten notes during lectures. Writing things down helps consolidate understanding and provides a valuable reference for future review. Having a pen and paper ready for each session is strongly recommended.

3.8 Practical Considerations: Security and Data Handling
When building real-world applications, especially those handling sensitive data, several important considerations emerge:
Working with Confidential Data
For projects involving confidential data (such as medical records), the recommendation is to:
Start with sample data or synthetic data during development
Focus on building and testing the functionality first
Only add security measures and real data once the basic system works
Implement proper authentication methods before deployment
Security has its own learning curve, and trying to learn everything at once can be overwhelming. The modular approach taught in this course allows you to add security measures after you understand the core functionality.
Alternative Approaches for Data Privacy
For organizations that can't share data with services like GPT:
Use open source models running locally on your PC
Implement proper authentication layers
Use encryption and secure data handling practices

The long-term goal is not to automate everything at once, but to incrementally improve your productivity. The recommendation is to automate approximately 10% of your tasks each month or quarter, depending on your bandwidth.

The Compound Effect
By consistently automating small portions of your workflow, you create a compound effect:
Month 1: 10% more productive
Month 3: 30% more productive
Month 6: 60% more productive
Year 1: Dramatically more productive than your peers
This incremental approach is sustainable and prevents burnout from trying to automate everything at once.

Lecture 4: Building APIs with FastAPI
This summary is meant to help you folks review or catch up on the session. It captures the key ideas and practical insights shared during this introduction to building backend APIs.

1. What Was Covered
This lecture shifted focus from consuming APIs to building your own using Python and the FastAPI framework. Key topics included:
APIs as Communication Protocols:
 Reinforced the idea that APIs define how different software components (like a frontend and a backend) interact.


FastAPI Introduction:
 Why FastAPI is widely used — performance, ease of use, automatic docs, and reliance on Python type hints.


Pydantic for Data Validation:
 FastAPI uses Pydantic models to define and validate the structure of incoming and outgoing data.


Defining API Endpoints:
 Using decorators like @app.post("/path") to tie specific URLs to Python functions.


Request and Response Handling:
 How clients send data (requests) to an API, and how the API returns structured responses (usually in JSON).


Uvicorn for Serving:
 FastAPI apps run on an ASGI server like Uvicorn, especially during local development.


CRUD Operations Mapping:
 Linked common database actions to HTTP methods:


POST → Create
GET → Read
PUT/PATCH → Update
DELETE → Delete
Practical Example – Slide Generation API:
 A conceptual walkthrough of a backend service that:


Takes research queries
Calls external APIs (e.g., SerpAPI, Grok Cloud)
Generates presentation slides using python-pptx

2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
Backend API Development:
 Focused on creating APIs instead of just using them. These APIs power applications by exposing business logic over HTTP.


FastAPI for Performance & Simplicity:
 Chosen for:
Built-in validation
Speed (async support)
Automatic docs via Swagger and ReDoc


Data Validation with Pydantic:
 Enforces data consistency via type-safe models. This means:
Errors are caught early
Clean, informative error messages
Less manual error checking


Asynchronous Programming (async def):
 Enables efficient handling of concurrent tasks (e.g., API calls or database reads) without blocking other requests.


Frontend/Backend Decoupling:
 Building an API backend allows multiple frontends (web, mobile, etc.) to communicate with it over HTTP, promoting modular architecture.


Auto-generated API Docs:
 FastAPI creates interactive documentation at:
/docs (Swagger UI)
/redoc (ReDoc)
 This helps others (or your future self) understand and test the API.

3. Tools & Frameworks Introduced 
FastAPI: Core web framework used to define the API.


Pydantic: Used for data validation and defining structured request/response models.
Uvicorn: The server that runs FastAPI apps (uvicorn main:app --reload).
Python: Language for implementing API logic.
Cursor: AI-powered IDE that helped generate and debug FastAPI code.
Streamlit / Gradio: Mentioned as possible frontends that would consume FastAPI services.
SerpAPI & Grok Cloud: External APIs called by the example backend for content and summarization.
python-pptx: Python library used to generate PowerPoint slides from backend logic.
4. Implementation Insights 
Basic FastAPI App Structure (main.py):
from fastapi import FastAPI
from pydantic import BaseModel
import uvicorn
#Define request and response models
class InputData(BaseModel):
    query: str
    max_results: int = 5
class OutputData(BaseModel):
    status: str
    results: list = []
# Create FastAPI app instance
app = FastAPI()

# Define POST endpoint
@app.post("/process-data/", response_model=OutputData)
async def process_data_endpoint(data: InputData):
    # Placeholder logic
    return OutputData(status="success", results=["result1", "result2"])
# Root endpoint
@app.get("/")
async def root():
    return {"message": "Welcome to my FastAPI!"}

# To run: uvicorn main:app --reload


Running Your FastAPI Server:
Navigate to your project directory in the terminal.


Run the command:
 uvicorn main:app --reload

Breakdown:
main → name of the file (main.py)
app → the FastAPI instance
--reload → enables hot-reloading during development

Accessing API Documentation:
Once the server is running (default: http://127.0.0.1:8000), you can access:
Swagger UI: http://127.0.0.1:8000/docs

ReDoc UI: http://127.0.0.1:8000/redoc

Conceptual Slide Generation API:
/research (POST):
 Accepts a topic → queries SerpAPI and Grok Cloud → returns summarized content.

/create_slides (POST):
 Takes that content → uses python-pptx to generate a PowerPoint file → returns confirmation or download link.

5. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: Why use Pydantic? Can’t I just use Python dictionaries?
A: You can, but Pydantic:
Validates data automatically using type hints

Returns structured, helpful error messages

Powers FastAPI’s auto-docs

Reduces boilerplate validation code
Q: What does async def do in my FastAPI endpoints?
A: It enables non-blocking behavior. For example, when calling external APIs or querying databases, your server can continue handling other requests without waiting. This improves performance and scalability.
Q: How would a frontend (like Streamlit) talk to this FastAPI backend?
A: The frontend would:
Use an HTTP client (like requests in Python or fetch in JavaScript)
Send a request to your FastAPI endpoint (e.g., http://localhost:8000/process-data/
Pass data in the request (e.g., JSON for POST)
Receive and render the response

Instructors introduced themselves and the program (Applied AI Lab/No-Code track).
Attendance, engagement, and participation rules were explained (turning on cameras, active contribution).
The cohort structure (live lectures, office hours, assignments) was discussed.
Focus was on building with no-code tools, project-based learning, and collaboration.
Students asked questions about requirements, content design, and program flow.
organizers emphasized discipline, teamwork, and completing assignments as core to success.
 In short: It was an introductory orientation + setup session, covering expectations, participation norms, and an overview of the program flow.

Lecture 5: Introduction to Databases & Domain Modeling, ERD
This session we explored the ‘fundamentals of databases’ and the critical role of ‘domain modeling’ in designing them. Through conceptual insights and hands-on exercises, mentees learned how to translate real-world problems into structured data models.

1.  What Was Covered
Beyond “Just Storage”: A database should act as the closest representation of truth for the real-world domain it serves (e.g., inventory, banking, education).


The Core Problem: How do we structure messy real-world information into a format that computers can understand and applications can rely on?


Domain Modeling: A key process to solve this. It's about creating a shared conceptual blueprint of the system's data, entities, and rules.


Building Blocks:
Entities: The main “things” (nouns) we care about – e.g., Book, Customer.
Attributes: Characteristics of entities – e.g., title, name, email.
Relationships: How entities interact – e.g., Student enrolls in Course.


Types of Relationships:
One-to-One (1:1)
One-to-Many (1:N)
Many-to-Many (M:N)


Hands-On Domain Modeling:
Mentees modeled domains like a library or car rental system using a shared spreadsheet.
Focus: Identify entities, their attributes, and their relationships.


Spreadsheet Simulation:
Each entity = a separate sheet/tab.
Each attribute = a column.
Simulate real-world data and operations to validate the model.
This leads to understanding Primary Keys (PKs) and Foreign Keys (FKs).

2.  Key Concepts & Ideas
Databases = Accurate Models of Reality
 A well-modeled database mirrors the real-world system it represents, making it trustworthy and effective.


Domain Modeling = Blueprint for the Database
 Like architecture for buildings. Prevents poor design, saves time, and improves clarity across teams.


How to Identify Key Elements:
Entities → Main nouns in your domain.
Attributes → Descriptive info about entities.
Relationships → Verbs linking entities.


Cardinality Matters
 Understanding 1:1, 1:N, M:N relationships is essential for table design and maintaining data integrity.


Primary Keys (PKs)
 Unique identifiers for each row in a table (e.g., StudentID).


Foreign Keys (FKs)
 Links between entities – allow joining related data efficiently.


Modeling is Iterative
 Don’t expect the first version to be perfect – test, refine, and improve.

3.  Tools & Frameworks
Google Sheets / Excel:
 Used to prototype databases before any code:
One tab = one entity
Columns = attributes
Rows = records
Helps identify PKs and FKs early


SQL (Mentioned Briefly):
 The language used to build and query databases (covered in depth in the BigBinary resource).


Supabase (Previewed):
 Open-source backend platform powered by PostgreSQL – alternative to Firebase.


4.  Implementation Insights
Spreadsheet Simulation Process:
Create a sheet for each entity.
Define attributes as columns.
Add sample rows of data.
Identify a Primary Key for each sheet.
Define Foreign Keys to link entities (e.g., StudentID in Enrollments referencing Students).
Create junction tables for M:N relationships (e.g., Enrollments between Students and Courses).
Test real use cases (e.g., enrolling a student, finding all their courses).


Example: Library System
Books Sheet → BookID (PK), Title, AuthorName, ISBN
Members Sheet → MemberID (PK), Name, Address
Loans Sheet → LoanID (PK), BookID (FK), MemberID (FK), DateBorrowed, DateDue

5.  Common Mentee Questions
Q: Why not skip domain modeling and just create tables in SQL or Supabase?
 → For complex systems, skipping modeling leads to inefficient, redundant, and hard-to-maintain databases. Domain modeling is like drafting a blueprint – it ensures structure, clarity, and long-term maintainability.
Q: When should something be an entity vs. just an attribute? (e.g., Author)
 → Depends on usage:
If you only need the author's name, it can be a simple attribute in Books.


If you need more info (bio, list of books, etc.), model Author as a separate entity and link it via a relationship.


Lecture 6: Connecting the Dots & Supabase (SQL) (Practical)
 This summary is meant to help you folks review or catch up on the session. It captures the key ideas and practical insights shared during this lecture on integrating a robust backend service.

This session was a crucial “connecting the dots” lecture, bridging our full-stack application journey from basic setups to production-ready architectures. The highlight was Supabase.

Deployed on Hugging Face Spaces

CSV file-based data storage
Also deployed on Hugging Face Spaces


Introducing Supabase – Our Level 3 Backend Solution
Open-source alternative to Firebase


Provides:
Managed PostgreSQL database
Built-in user authentication
Auto-generated APIs and more


Setting Up Supabase
Create a project via the Supabase dashboard


Locate your:
Project URL
API Keys:
anon key (public)
service_role key (private – backend only)


Store API keys in environment variables (.env file)


Defining the Database Schema
Based on domain modeling (e.g., customers table for AI CRM)


Create tables/columns using:
GUI
SQL Editor

Modify FastAPI app to replace CSV storage with Supabase (using supabase-py)


User Authentication with Supabase
Built-in support for sign-up/login


Integrated into the Streamlit frontend


Why Supabase?
Scalable, secure, managed backend


Features like Row-Level Security (RLS)


Real-World Examples
Perplexity AI, Cursor, ChatBase = successful “LLM wrapper” apps using similar architectures

2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
Full-Stack Architecture Evolution
Level 1: All-in-one file


Level 2: Modular, API-based


Level 3: Scalable backend (Supabase)


Supabase as a BaaS
Offers PostgreSQL + Auth + File Storage + Real-time Updates


Frees you to focus on frontend/business logic


Persistent, Scalable Data Storage
Benefits of Supabase/PostgreSQL:
Data persistence
Scalability
Data integrity
Efficient querying
Secure User Authentication
Supabase handles it via email/password, social logins


Easy integration into apps

Fine-grained access control


Users can only access their own data

Store keys in .env, never commit them


Use platform-secured secrets for deployed apps

4. Implementation Insights 
Setting Up Supabase Project
Create project on supabase.com
Get:
Project URL
API Keys
anon (client-safe)
service_role (server-only)
Add to .env:
SUPABASE_URL="your_project_url"
SUPABASE_KEY="your_service_role_key"


Creating Tables
Use Table Editor GUI or SQL:


CREATE TABLE customers (
    id BIGINT PRIMARY KEY GENERATED ALWAYS AS IDENTITY,
    name TEXT,
    email TEXT UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    phone_number TEXT,
    lead_score REAL,
    created_at TIMESTAMPTZ DEFAULT NOW()
);

FastAPI Integration

Install dependencies:
 pip install supabase python-dotenv


Initialize client:
from supabase import create_client, Client
from dotenv import load_dotenv
import os

load_dotenv()

SUPABASE_URL = os.environ.get("SUPABASE_URL")
SUPABASE_KEY = os.environ.get("SUPABASE_KEY")
supabase_client = create_client(SUPABASE_URL, SUPABASE_KEY)

Example: Insert a customer:
 response = supabase_client.table("customers").insert(customer_data).execute()

Streamlit Authentication
Enable email provider in Supabase


Sign-Up (Streamlit code snippet):

 user_response = supabase_client.auth.sign_up({
 	"email": email,
	"password": password
  })


Login:

 session_response = supabase_client.auth.sign_in_with_password({
	"email": email,
	"password": password})

use st.session_state to manage login state

5. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: Is Supabase free?
Yes! Generous free tier:
DB storage
Auth users
File storage
Edge functions
Great for MVPs and small projects

Q: Do I need to learn PostgreSQL deeply?
Not at first
Supabase’s GUI + Python library covers most needs
Basic SQL knowledge will help long term

Q: Which key to use – anon or service_role?
anon key: Use in client apps, respects RLS
service_role key: Use only on the backend, bypasses RLS – keep secret!

Lecture 7: Introduction to AI-Powered Development (VibeCode)
LESSON MAP
Core Question: How do AI coding tools amplify your existing FastAPI, Supabase, and prompt engineering knowledge to ship faster—without replacing your judgment?
The Starting Point:
Students have 8 weeks of FastAPI, Supabase, database design, and prompt engineering under their belt
They assume AI tools either write perfect code automatically or are just fancy autocomplete
They're curious but skeptical about whether these tools actually work for real projects
The Destination:
Students understand AI tools as amplifiers that require clear PRDs, technical context, and human judgment
They can execute a 9-step workflow from PRD to deployed app using Cursor, Lovable, and Claude Code
They recognize when to use which tool and how to debug AI-generated code using their existing knowledge
The Journey:
Opening scenario: Two demos—vague Bolt.new failure vs. specific V0 success (GIGO principle)
Landscape tour: Cursor Composer, Lovable, Claude Code, Gemini Pro 3, Bolt.new, V0 (tools as specialists)
First hands-on: Write micro-PRD with ChatGPT, generate UI in Lovable
Deep dive: Cursor with @codebase context, iterative refinement
Second hands-on: Clone, refine, test cycle
Workflow synthesis: The complete 9-step ship cycle
Third hands-on: Export, edit, commit workflow
Readiness check: "Who can ship this weekend?"
Key Concepts Covered:
AI tools as knowledge amplifiers vs. replacements
GIGO principle (garbage in, garbage out)
PRD-driven development with AI assist
Context provisioning (@codebase, @docs, @file in Cursor)
Tool specialization (Lovable for UI, Cursor for refinement, Claude Code for architecture)
The handoff workflow (cloud tool → local environment → version control)
Connections:
Builds on: Lecture 4 (FastAPI structure), Lecture 6 (Supabase integration), Lecture 7-8 (prompt engineering, structured outputs)
Sets up: Next week's MVP build sprint where students apply this full workflow to their projects
Depth & Scope:
Depth: Hands-on mastery—students will use 3+ tools during class and complete micro-project
Scope: Deliberately excluding IDE customization, tool-chasing, enterprise deployment, CI/CD pipelines, custom model training

PHASE 1: FOUNDATIONS & FIRST CONTACT (45 minutes)
Opening: The Tale of Two Demos
It's 11:47 PM on a Tuesday. You've been manually copy-pasting Discord messages into a Google Sheet for two hours—checking which students submitted Lecture 5's practice set. Your eyes hurt from switching between tabs. Your wrist aches from Ctrl+C, Ctrl+V. You think: "There has to be a better way."
You open Bolt.new. You've heard about AI coding tools. You type: "Build me a dashboard."
Thirty seconds later, you're staring at something. Gradients. A sidebar. Three chart types. Looks... professional? Almost too good. You click the big green button labeled "Submit Assignment."
Nothing happens.
You click again. Still nothing. You open the browser console. No errors. You check the code. There's a handleSubmit() function that's just empty. There's a database schema with a single JSON field called data storing everything—no foreign keys, no relationships, no structure. The authentication checks if user === 'user123'. It's hardcoded.
This isn't a dashboard. It's a movie set. It looks real from the outside, but there's nothing behind the walls.
Your first thought: "AI tools are garbage."
Question 1: What went wrong here?
Before I tell you what broke, take 10 seconds. What do you think the real problem was here? Not "the AI is bad"—what specifically did the person do wrong when they typed "Build me a dashboard"?
The real issue: The AI had zero context about your world.
It didn't know you already have a FastAPI backend from Lecture 4. It didn't know your Supabase schema from Lecture 6 with students, assignments, and submissions tables. It didn't know you've been following REST API patterns for eight weeks. It didn't know what "assignment submission" even means in your specific cohort workflow.
So it did what any reasonable system would do when given no context: it guessed. And its guesses were generic, safe, and useless.
This is the GIGO principle—Garbage In, Garbage Out. AI tools aren't mind readers. They're amplifiers. If you feed them vague instructions with no context, they amplify vagueness. If you feed them precise context, they amplify precision.
Now picture this instead: You open the same tool. But this time, you first write a half-page document:
Purpose: Track which students have submitted assignments for Lecture 5 practice set
Users: Instructors (view all submissions), Students (view only their own)
Data: Submissions table with student_id, assignment_id, submitted_at, file_url
Features: List view with filters, submission status badges, file preview
Tech stack: FastAPI backend, Supabase database, existing auth from Lecture 6 setup
Now you open Bolt.new. You paste this entire document into the chat. You also say: "Here's my current API structure" and attach your routes.py file. "Here's my Supabase schema" and share the SQL from Lecture 6.
Thirty seconds later, you have a dashboard that actually connects to your database, uses your auth patterns, and follows your API structure. The styling might be off. One endpoint might need async/await. The filter dropdowns might not have all edge cases handled.
But the bones are solid. It's 80% there instead of 0% there.
What changed? You provided context. You told the AI what you know. You gave it guard rails. The AI amplified your existing FastAPI and Supabase knowledge instead of guessing from scratch.
This is the real idea hiding underneath all of this: AI coding tools are not autonomous builders. They're collaborative partners that work best when you bring domain knowledge, technical context, and clear requirements.

The Landscape: Tools as Specialists
Before we dive into hands-on work, let's map the terrain. There are dozens of AI coding tools. We're not going to chase every new release. Instead, we're going to understand three categories and know which tool to reach for when.
Question 2: If all these tools use similar AI models under the hood, why do they feel so different?
Because they're optimized for different workflows.
Think of them as specialists in a dev team:
1. The UI Designers (Lovable, Bolt.new, V0)
Lovable: Drag-and-drop UI builder with chat interface. It generates React components, integrates with Supabase directly, and gives you a live preview. Major brands like Notion and smaller startups use it for rapid prototyping. You chat with it like: "Add a pricing page with three tiers" and it updates the UI in real-time.
V0 (by Vercel): Component-focused. You describe a component, it generates clean React code with Tailwind. Used heavily by frontend developers who want pixel-perfect UI without manual CSS tweaking.
Bolt.new: Similar to Lovable but more general-purpose. Good for quick experiments but less polished for production.
When to use them: Prototyping a frontend, generating landing pages, building admin dashboards. Essentially, when you need UI fast and care more about speed than custom polish.
2. The Code Refiners (Cursor, Windsurf)
Cursor: IDE built on VS Code with AI baked in. It has three killer features: (1) Composer for multi-file edits, (2) @mentions to give context (@codebase, @docs, @file), (3) Tab autocomplete that understands your patterns. Anthropic's team uses Cursor internally. Early-stage startups building products on Claude's API often use Cursor for rapid iteration.
Windsurf: Similar philosophy, slightly different interface. Some teams prefer it for its git integration.
When to use them: Refining AI-generated code, adding features to existing projects, debugging, writing tests. Essentially, when you have a codebase and need to extend or fix it intelligently.
3. The Architects (Claude Code, Gemini Pro 3 with Composer)
Claude Code (via Anthropic): Recent release that uses Claude 3.7 Sonnet specifically tuned for code generation. It excels at understanding entire project structures and suggesting architectural patterns. Real-world example: A developer at a Series A startup used Claude Code to refactor a monolithic FastAPI app into microservices—it suggested the service boundaries, wrote the inter-service API contracts, and generated migration scripts. The developer said: "It didn't just write code; it understood why the architecture needed to change."
Gemini Pro 3 (via Google AI Studio): Latest model from Google with massive context window (2 million tokens). A team building an e-commerce platform used Gemini Pro 3 to analyze their entire Supabase schema, 47 API routes, and front-end React components simultaneously, then suggest a normalized database restructure that reduced query complexity by 40%.
When to use them: Greenfield architecture decisions, large refactors, understanding how pieces fit together. Essentially, when you need strategy before tactics.


Question 3: Why can't one tool do everything?
It could, technically. But specialization leads to better UX. Lovable's chat interface wouldn't work well for debugging a 3,000-line FastAPI file. Cursor's IDE setup would be overkill for generating a landing page. Each tool makes trade-offs.
Our strategy today: Start with Lovable for UI (fast iteration), move to Cursor for refinement (real dev environment), call Claude Code when architecture questions arise (strategic guidance). You'll learn the handoff points.
Hands-On Activity 1: PRD + Lovable Prototype (15 minutes)
Let's get your hands dirty. You're going to build a micro-feature right now.
Step 1: Write a Micro-PRD (5 minutes)
Open ChatGPT (or Claude, or any LLM). Paste this persona prompt:
You are an expert product manager. Interview me about a feature I want to build. Ask me 5-6 questions to understand the purpose, users, data structure, and constraints. Then write a half-page PRD.

Hit enter. Answer its questions honestly. Think of something small—a feature for your existing FastAPI project, or a new component for your MVP. Could be:
"A dashboard showing my API request counts per endpoint"
"A form for students to submit assignments with file uploads"
"A settings page where users can update their notification preferences"
By the end, you should have a half-page document with:
Purpose
Users
Core features (3-5 bullet points)
Data structure notes
Technical constraints (uses FastAPI, connects to Supabase, etc.)
Step 2: Generate in Lovable (10 minutes)
Go to Lovable (link in cohort Discord). Start a new project. Paste your PRD into the chat.
Lovable will generate a UI. It might take 20-30 seconds.
Now iterate: Try these chat commands:
"Change the primary color to blue"
"Add a logo in the top-left corner"
"Make the form layout use two columns instead of one"
"Add a data table showing submission history below the form"
Watch how it updates in real-time. This is the iteration loop—chat, preview, refine.

Question 4: What breaks first when you iterate?
Usually styling inconsistencies or state management bugs. You'll notice a button that looks like it should submit, but doesn't. Or a table that renders static data instead of fetching from API.
That's expected. Lovable is optimized for speed, not perfection. Its job is to get you 70% of the way there fast. The remaining 30% is your job—and that's where Cursor comes in.
Before moving on, export your Lovable project to GitHub. Lovable has a button for this. It pushes the code to a new repo under your account. You'll need that for Phase 2.

Signal 1: The Interrupter During the earlier vague Bolt.new demo, someone thinks: "Wait, that database schema is wrong. A single JSON field for course modules isn't queryable. We'd need an enrollments table with foreign keys from Lecture 5." → Meaning: You're applying technical judgment, not impressed by pretty code.
Signal 2: The Process Question Right now, someone's thinking: "Can I show Lovable my existing Supabase schema so it doesn't guess? How do I tell it about my auth setup from Lecture 6?" → Meaning: You're reverse-engineering inputs, not just admiring outputs. You're planning to replicate this independently.
Signal 3: The Comparison Question Someone asks: "When would I use Lovable instead of Cursor Composer for UI? If Claude Code is better for architecture, why bother with Cursor?" → Meaning: You're building a decision tree. You're thinking about tool selection based on use cases.
Signal 4: The "I Want to Try Now" Moment During this 15-minute activity, I see students who:
Write a plan before touching Lovable (PRD thinking)
Reference old code with @file mentions (context provisioning)
Iterate intelligently ("Change this specific thing" not "Make it better")
Ask: "How do I connect this to my existing FastAPI routes?" not "Why doesn't it work?" → Meaning: You're ready to ship. You're not waiting for perfect instructions.

PHASE 2: THE SHIP CYCLE & READINESS 
Deep Dive: Cursor + Context = Smart Code
You've generated a UI in Lovable. It looks decent. But here's the gap: Lovable doesn't know about your existing FastAPI setup, your Supabase schema from Lecture 6, or the API patterns you've been following.
Question 5: How do you bridge the cloud tool (Lovable) to your local development reality?
This is where Cursor becomes your refinery.
Clone the Lovable repo locally. Open it in Cursor. Now, before you ask Cursor to do anything, you need to give it context. This is the critical step most people skip.
The @mention System: Teaching Cursor About Your World
Cursor has three killer features for context:
@codebase - Tells Cursor to scan your entire project structure
@docs - Point it to API documentation (FastAPI docs, Supabase docs)
@file - Reference specific files like your existing routes.py
Real-world example: Anthropic's Security Engineering team transformed their workflow using Claude Code (which powers Cursor). They went from "design doc → janky code → refactor → give up on tests" to asking for pseudocode first, guiding through test-driven development, and checking in periodically. Result: more reliable, testable code. One engineer said: "What used to require an hour of Google searching and reading docs now takes 10-20 minutes—an 80% reduction in research time."
The secret wasn't that Claude wrote perfect code. It was that they gave it context through @mentions and guided it iteratively.
Try this now (Hands-On Activity 2 - 15 minutes):
Open your cloned Lovable project in Cursor. In Composer mode (Cmd+K or Ctrl+K), type:
@codebase Look at the current API structure. I have an existing FastAPI backend with these endpoints: [paste your routes]. Refactor the frontend API calls to match my existing patterns. Use async/await consistently.

Watch Cursor work. It will:
Scan your codebase
Identify where API calls are made
Suggest changes that align with your existing structure
Show you a diff before applying
You can accept, reject, or iterate: "Actually, use axios instead of fetch for consistency."

Question 6: But what if you need architectural guidance—like deciding whether to split your monolithic app into microservices?
This is where Claude Code and Gemini Pro 2.0 shine. They're the architects, not the code refiners.
Claude Code example: A developer at a Series A startup used Claude Code to refactor a monolithic FastAPI app into microservices. The tool didn't just write code—it understood why the architecture needed to change. It suggested service boundaries, wrote inter-service API contracts, and generated migration scripts. The developer said: "It understood the strategic decision, not just the tactical implementation."
Gemini Pro 2.0 example: A team building an e-commerce platform used Gemini Pro 2.0's 2 million token context window to analyze their entire Supabase schema (47 tables), all API routes, and frontend React components simultaneously. It suggested a normalized database restructure that reduced query complexity by 40%. That's the power of massive context windows—you can feed it your entire system and ask: "What's the bottleneck here?"
When to call the architect:
Greenfield: "Should this be a monolith or microservices?"
Refactor: "How do I migrate this database schema without downtime?"
Scale: "Which parts of this system will break at 10x traffic?"

The Complete 9-Step Ship Cycle
By end of today, if I ask: "Walk me through your process," here's what you should be able to say:
1. PRD with LLM assist
 "I open ChatGPT, use a persona prompt—'You're an expert PM'—and it interviews me about my app. I answer 5-6 questions. Out comes a half-page PRD."
2. Generate in Lovable
 "I paste that PRD into Lovable. It spits out a UI. I iterate with chat: 'add this logo,' 'change colors to match my brand,' 'integrate Supabase auth.'"
Real-world validation: Lovable reached $17M in annual recurring revenue with 500,000 users building 25,000 new products daily. One user built a $30K app in just 10 days with minimal coding experience. Another raised $500K for an AI tool built entirely through Lovable. These aren't outliers—they're the pattern when you combine clear PRDs with rapid iteration tools.
3. Test in cloud
 "I preview it right there in Lovable. Does it work? Do the buttons do what they should? If not, I refine the prompt."
4. Export to GitHub
 "Once it's solid, I export. Lovable pushes it to my GitHub repo."
5. Clone and open in Cursor
 "I clone that repo locally. Open it in Cursor. Now I'm in my real dev environment."
6. Refine with Cursor
 "I use @codebase to give Cursor context. I use Composer to add features. I guide it: 'Use this API pattern from my docs.' I fix what it breaks."
Anthropic's Product Design team insight: They feed Figma design files to Claude Code, set up autonomous loops where it writes code, runs tests, and iterates continuously. They give it abstract problems, let it work autonomously, then review solutions before final refinements. In one case, they had Claude build Vim key bindings for itself with minimal human review. One engineer noted: "We're making large state management changes that you typically wouldn't see a designer making."
7. Add tests
 "I ask Cursor: 'Write tests for this endpoint.' It generates them. I run them. I fix what fails."
8. Version control
 "I commit as I go. Small commits. 'Added user dashboard,' 'Fixed auth bug,' 'Added tests.'"
9. Deploy
 "I push to Vercel or Render. App is live."



Question 7: What does "readiness" actually mean here?
It's not about memorizing these 9 steps. It's about understanding the PRD → AI → Review cycle. It's knowing which tool to open when. It's feeling confident enough to try and fail without panicking.
The Three Must-Dos Before You Leave
Before anyone walks out, you need to have actually done these three things:
1. Written a micro-PRD using ChatGPT with a persona prompt (even if it's just for a single feature)
This can be as simple as: "I want to add a 'delete account' button to my settings page." Run it through the ChatGPT interview. Get a structured output. This proves you understand that AI tools need clear inputs.
2. Generated something in Lovable OR Cursor and iterated on it at least once
The iteration is the key. Don't accept the first output. Say: "Make the button bigger," or "Use my existing color scheme," or "Add error handling." This proves you understand AI as a collaborator, not a magic wand.
3. Exported/opened your code locally and made one manual edit to understand the handoff
Open the code in VS Code or Cursor. Change a single line—maybe a button label, maybe an API endpoint. Commit it. This proves you understand the code is yours to control, not locked inside the AI tool.
Why these three matter: If you've done them during class, you can repeat the full 9-step cycle on your own tonight. You're not dependent on the instructor. You're ready to ship.
Final Readiness Check: No Laptop, Just Explanation
Close your laptop. No notes.
Turn to the person next to you. Explain this:
"You get a feature request Monday morning: 'Build a page where instructors can see all pending assignment reviews.' You have 2 hours. Walk me through your process. Where do you start? What tools do you open? What do you type?"
If you can explain this in 60 seconds without checking notes, you're ready.
If you can't, stop me now and ask—because the goal today isn't to impress me with how much you remember. It's to make you dangerous by Monday morning.

Success indicators:
You can explain the workflow without referencing notes
You've completed all three must-dos
You understand which tool solves which problem
You're not waiting for perfect instructions—you're ready to try and iterate
The Goal: Readiness, Not Perfection

Question 8: Do you need to be a Cursor expert by the end of today?
No. You need to:
Understand the PRD → AI → Review cycle
Know which tool to open when (Lovable for UI speed, Cursor for refinement, Claude Code for architecture)
Feel confident enough to try and fail without panicking
Connect your past 8 weeks of learning (FastAPI, Supabase, prompts) to these new tools
Remember back in Lecture 4 when we built FastAPI routes? That knowledge isn't obsolete—it's what lets you guide Cursor intelligently. Remember Lecture 6's Supabase setup? That's what lets you spot when Lovable generates a schema that won't work for your use case.
Real perspective from Anthropic's engineering team: They pushed 60-100 internal releases per day with ~5 pull requests per developer daily (compared to industry standard of 1-2). The secret? Not perfect AI usage—it's rapid iteration with AI as an amplifier. One engineer built around 20 prototypes of a new feature in just a few hours over two days using Claude Code. That's the goal: speed of experimentation, not perfection of execution.
Gemini 3 Flash reality check: Previously, generating functional code in a single pass was a job for Pro models. Gemini 2.5 Flash often struggled with complexity, resulting in broken logic. Now Gemini 3 Flash handles these tasks with precision, but it's still 3x faster at a fraction of the cost. The point? Even the "fast" tools require your judgment to refine the output.
If you're experiencing these signals, next week will feel natural:
Signal Check (Revisited):
The Interrupter: You spotted technical flaws during demos
The Process Question: You asked about PRDs, context provisioning, handoff points
The Comparison Question: You're building a decision tree for tool selection
The "I Want to Try Now" Moment: You've been building during activities, not just watching
If you're experiencing these, you're ready to ship. If not, ask questions now—the goal isn't to rush through demos. The goal is readiness to build this weekend.

What's Next
We've stayed in a single developer's workflow today. Next week, we're doing a 120-minute build sprint where you'll apply this full workflow to your own MVP projects. You'll arrive with a PRD written, Cursor configured, and realistic expectations.
Between now and then: Try extending one feature in your existing FastAPI project using this workflow. Document what worked and what didn't. Ping Discord if you get stuck—but struggle with it first. The struggle is where the learning happens.
The real test isn't whether you finish perfectly. It's whether you start building tonight without waiting for perfect instructions.

End of Lecture. Homework: Install Cursor, write one PRD, build one feature using the 9-step cycle. Bring questions to next week's sprint.



Lecture 8: MVP Building Workshop
LESSON MAP
Core Question / Big Idea How does the code you've been writing on localhost become a live app with a shareable URL—and why is the gap smaller than you think?
The Starting Point Students have built Gradio UIs, FastAPI backends, connected Supabase, and used AI tools (Lovable/Cursor). But they treat localhost demos as "practice" and assume deployment requires Docker, AWS, or weeks of "productionizing." They're waiting to learn deployment as a separate skill.
The Destination Students realize deployment isn't a new skill—it's connecting pieces they already know through git + platform. By end of session, every student has a live URL they can share, understanding that their localhost code IS production code.
The Journey
Open with 5-minute live deploy demo (crack the assumption)
Connect to Lecture 9's GitHub export workflow
First build cycle: Deploy existing Lovable project (30 min)
Troubleshoot the Big Three errors (port, requirements, env vars)
Second build cycle: Push update, see auto-redeploy (20 min)
Final check: Live URLs in Discord, students testing each other's apps
Key Concepts Covered
Localhost = production (same code, different server)
Git as deployment trigger (not manual upload)
Environment variables (local .env → platform secrets)
Platform auto-detection (Render/Railway read your repo structure)
Common deployment errors and fixes
The ship cycle: code → commit → push → deploy
Connections
Builds on: Lecture 4 (FastAPI structure), Lecture 6 (Supabase config), Lecture 9 (Lovable → GitHub export, Cursor refinement)
Sets up: Week 11 capstone where students ship complete MVPs independently
Depth & Scope
Depth: Hands-on deployment mastery—students troubleshoot errors, configure platforms, understand logs
Scope: Deliberately skip Docker, CI/CD pipelines, custom domains, scaling/load balancing, staging environments

PHASE 1: THE 5-MINUTE CRACK
It's Monday morning. You open Discord. Priya posted over the weekend: "Finished the Lovable exercise from Lecture 7. Got the FastAPI connection working in Cursor. What should I do with it?"
Most students think the answer is: "Add more features. Polish the UI. Wait for the deployment lecture."
What would you tell Priya right now? Take 10 seconds. Would you say: (a) keep building locally until it's perfect, or (b) ship what you have?
The real answer: Ship it. Today. In the next 5 minutes.
Let me show you why.

Live Demo: Localhost to Live in 300 Seconds
I pull up Priya's GitHub repo (the one she exported from Lovable last week). It's sitting there:
FastAPI backend (main.py, routes from Lecture 4 patterns)
Gradio frontend (chat interface from Lecture 2)
Supabase connection (setup from Lecture 6)
All working on localhost:8000
Before I start the timer, quick question: What do you think will break first when we deploy Priya's localhost code to production? Take 10 seconds. Write it down. 
Minute 1: Open Render.com (or Railway). Click "New Web Service."
Minute 2: Connect GitHub. Select Priya's repo. Render reads your requirements.txt and installs those exact versions. This is the contract: "My app needs these libraries." Render says: "I'll install them before running your code."

Without requirements.txt, Render guesses. Guessing breaks things. The file isn't optional—it's how your code tells the platform what it depends on.
Minute 3: Set environment variables. Copy-paste from her .env:
SUPABASE_URL=https://your-project.supabase.co
SUPABASE_KEY=eyJhbG...
GROQ_API_KEY=gsk_...
Minute 4: Click "Deploy." Render builds the app (installs dependencies, starts server).
Minute 5: App is live. URL: https://priya-submission-tracker.onrender.com
You click it. Gradio interface loads. I test the form. It submits to FastAPI backend. Data shows up in Supabase dashboard.
Nothing broke. So what did you predict wrong?
What changed between localhost and production?
Nothing. Same code. Same database. Same API patterns from Lecture 4.

Question: If the code is identical, what assumption just broke?
The assumption that "deployment" is a separate skill you haven't learned yet.
You've been writing production-ready code since Week 4. The gap wasn't technical. It was mental.

The Kavya Example: When Students Realize They Were Ready
Kavya from Cohort 4 built an internal tool during Week 10:
Slack bot querying customer support tickets

Deployed on Render

PHASE 2: YOUR FIRST DEPLOY
Build Cycle 1: Ship Your Lovable Project (30 minutes)
You already have code ready to deploy. Last week's Lovable export from Lecture 9. Let's ship it.
Step 1: Verify GitHub Export (2 minutes)
Open your GitHub profile. Find the repo Lovable created last week. Should have:
your-app/
├── main.py (FastAPI routes)
├── requirements.txt (dependencies)
├── .env.example (template)
└── frontend/ (Gradio or React UI)

If it's not there, export from Lovable now: Settings → Export to GitHub.

Step 2: Create Render Account (3 minutes)
Go to render.com (or railway.app if you prefer). Sign in with GitHub. This gives Render access to your repos.
Why these platforms? They read your requirements.txt, detect Python apps automatically, and handle server config. You don't touch Docker.

Step 3: Deploy Setup (10 minutes)
Click "New Web Service" → Select your Lovable repo.
Render shows a form. Fill it:
Build Command: (leave default or use)
pip install -r requirements.txt

Start Command:
uvicorn main:app --host 0.0.0.0 --port $PORT

Key detail: Notice $PORT not :8000. Render assigns a random port. Your app must listen to it.
This is Common Error #1. If you hardcode :8000, deploy succeeds but app crashes. Check this in your main.py:
# ❌ Wrong (hardcoded port)
if __name__ == "__main__":
    uvicorn.run(app, host="0.0.0.0", port=8000)

# ✅ Right (reads from environment)
if __name__ == "__main__":
    import os
    port = int(os.getenv("PORT", 8000))
    uvicorn.run(app, host="0.0.0.0", port=port)

Fix this before deploying.

Step 4: Environment Variables (5 minutes)
Scroll to "Environment Variables" section. Add these from your .env:
SUPABASE_URL = https://your-project.supabase.co
SUPABASE_KEY = eyJhbGciOiJ...
GROQ_API_KEY = gsk_...

Common Error #2: Typo in Supabase URL. Missing https://. Copy exact string from Supabase dashboard.

Step 5: Deploy (5 minutes)
Click "Create Web Service."
Watch the logs. You'll see:
Installing dependencies from requirements.txt...
✓ Installed fastapi, uvicorn, supabase, groq...
Starting server...
✓ Application startup complete

When it says "Live," copy the URL. Click it. Test your app.

Step 6: Test & Debug (5 minutes)
Your app loads. Try a form submission. Does it work?
If yes: Paste URL in Discord. You're done with Cycle 1.
If no: Check logs. Common errors:
Error: "Module not found" Fix: Missing package in requirements.txt. Add it:
pip freeze > requirements.txt
git add requirements.txt
git commit -m "Add missing dependency"
git push

Render auto-redeploys on push.
Error: "Connection to Supabase failed" Fix: Check environment variables. Are they exact matches from your .env? One wrong character breaks it.
Error: "Port already in use" Fix: Check main.py for hardcoded :8000. Use $PORT instead.

Question: What did you just learn that you couldn't learn from reading docs?
How deployment actually feels. The 30-second panic when logs scroll past. The relief when "Live" appears. The frustration of a typo breaking everything.
This is the gap between knowing and doing.

PHASE 3: THE SHIP CYCLE
Build Cycle 2: Update & Auto-Redeploy (20 minutes)
Your app is live. Now change something.
Step 1: Clone Locally (if you haven't)
git clone https://github.com/your-username/your-app.git
cd your-app

Open in Cursor or VS Code.

Step 2: Make a Small Change (5 minutes)
Pick one:
Change a button label in your Gradio UI
Add a new API endpoint in main.py
Update error message text
Example: Add a simple health check
@app.get("/health")
def health_check():
    return {"status": "healthy", "timestamp": datetime.now()}

Test locally:
uvicorn main:app --reload

Open localhost:8000/health. Works? Good.

Step 3: Commit & Push (3 minutes)
git add .
git commit -m "Add health check endpoint"
git push

Question: What happens next without you doing anything?
Render detects the push. Rebuilds your app. Redeploys automatically.
Watch the Render dashboard. Logs start flowing. 30 seconds later, your change is live.
Visit your-app.onrender.com/health. See your new endpoint.

This is the ship cycle:
Code locally
Test on localhost
Commit to git
Push to GitHub
Platform auto-deploys
Test live URL
No manual upload. No FTP. No server SSH. Just git.

PHASE 4: TROUBLESHOOTING & READINESS
The Big Three Deployment Errors (Everyone Hits These)
Error 1: Port Binding
# Breaks on Render
uvicorn.run(app, port=8000)

# Works on Render
port = int(os.getenv("PORT", 8000))
uvicorn.run(app, host="0.0.0.0", port=port)

Error 2: Missing Dependencies
# Check what's installed locally
pip freeze

# Compare to requirements.txt
cat requirements.txt

# Missing package? Add it
echo "python-multipart" >> requirements.txt
git commit -am "Add missing dependency"
git push

Error 3: Environment Variable Typos Check Render dashboard → Environment → Variables. Compare letter-by-letter to your .env. One wrong character = broken app.

Reading Logs (The Debugging Skill That Matters)
When deploy fails, click "Logs" in Render dashboard. Look for:
ModuleNotFoundError: No module named 'groq'
→ Missing from requirements.txt

sqlalchemy.exc.OperationalError: connection refused
→ Wrong Supabase URL in env vars

Address already in use: 0.0.0.0:8000
→ Hardcoded port, need $PORT

Practice: Intentionally break something, read the logs, fix it.
This builds debugging muscle memory.

 ✅ Fix port binding errors
 ✅ Read logs to diagnose issues
 ✅ Push updates via git and see auto-redeploy
 ✅ Test live app and confirm it matches localhost behavior
Question: Can you walk me through deploying your Week 4 FastAPI project right now without notes?
If yes, you're ready to ship your MVP independently.
If no, what's blocking you? Ask now. We'll debug together.

Lecture 9: Introduction to LLMs & Prompt Engineering
This summary helps you folks review or catch up on key insights from our foundational session on Large Language Models (LLMs). We explored what LLMs are, how they work, and how to interact with them effectively using prompt engineering.

1. What Was Covered 
This session laid the groundwork for understanding how modern AI systems process and generate language. We traced the evolution from traditional Machine Learning to advanced LLMs and highlighted the shift toward using these models via instruction-following prompts.
Key Topics:
The AI Hierarchy:
AI → Machine Learning → Deep Learning → Large Language Models (LLMs).
We used music genre classification as a tangible example of classic ML.


Why Traditional ML Falls Short for Language: It struggles with the complexity, ambiguity, and nuance of natural language.


Deep Learning & Transformers: DL enables automatic feature extraction; transformers use attention mechanisms to understand context across long texts.


What GPT Stands For:
Generative – Predicts and creates new text.
Pre-trained – Learns from massive text datasets.
Transformer – Neural architecture that powers the model’s contextual understanding.


From GPT-2 to ChatGPT:
Shift from text completion to instruction following via:
Instruction Fine-tuning
RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback)


In-Context Learning (ICL): LLMs can adapt on the fly based on prompt examples — no retraining required.


Word Embeddings: Words are stored as numerical vectors, capturing semantic meaning.

2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
LLMs as Pattern Learners: At scale, LLMs recognize and generate patterns in human language with surprising fluency.


Pre-training & Scale: Pre-training on massive, diverse datasets enables general world knowledge and contextual awareness.


Instruction Following: Transformed LLMs into versatile assistants that respond to prompts like “Explain X” or “Summarize Y.”


RLHF for Alignment: Helps make LLMs more helpful, harmless, and aligned with human values.


Few-Shot Prompting: Show the model a few examples in the prompt, and it can mimic the pattern or style immediately.


Emergent Capabilities: Abilities like reasoning and ICL seem to “emerge” when models get big enough — not explicitly programmed, but learned through scale.


From Word Prediction to Task Execution: LLMs evolved from just filling in text to carrying out multi-step tasks intelligently.

3. Tools & Frameworks Introduced 
Hugging Face Model Hub: Browse and test pre-trained LLMs like GPT-2.


Groq Cloud Playground: Interactive environment to test prompts with open source LLMs (like LLaMA).


TensorFlow Embedding Projector: Visualize word embeddings and understand how models see semantic similarity.


Claude (by Anthropic): Real-world use case: generating script content using style-based prompting via ICL.

4. Implementation Insights 
Though not a coding session, we explored real-time interactions with LLMs using playgrounds:
🆚 GPT-2 vs ChatGPT:
GPT-2 treats inputs like completion tasks (e.g., “Write a joke about a cat…” → may not actually tell a joke).


ChatGPT/LLaMA responds to instructions directly, showcasing instruction-following behavior.


💡 In-Context Learning Activity:
Folks provided samples of their writing (e.g., LinkedIn posts).


Prompted the LLM to analyze the writing style.


Asked it to generate new content in that style.


Result: The LLM successfully mimicked tone, structure, and vocabulary without any retraining — just through prompt design.


System prompt in Groq Cloud was used to set the LLM’s persona or context, while examples + instructions went into the user message.

5. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: How do LLMs seem so smart if they’re just predicting the next word?
 A: Scale is everything. With enough data and model capacity, LLMs learn deep relationships, reasoning patterns, and task strategies — making them appear intelligent even though the core mechanism is statistical prediction.
Q: What's the difference between pre-training and fine-tuning?
 A:
Pre-training: Trains a model from scratch on general text (massive data, expensive).


Fine-tuning: Adapts a pre-trained model for a specific use case (e.g., medical Q&A, instruction-following).
 As developers, we usually work with already pre-trained models and adapt behavior via prompt engineering.
Q: Does In-Context Learning change the model permanently?
 A: No. It only affects the current interaction. The model uses your examples to adapt 
temporarily — once the session ends, that context is gone.
Q: Why does scaling up the model unlock new capabilities like ICL?
 A: Large models can memorize and generalize complex patterns better. At a certain scale, surprising capabilities (like reasoning or few-shot learning) “emerge” naturally — not from new code, but from more capacity and training data.


Lecture 10: Prompt Engineering & Building LLM Wrappers

This summary is meant to help you folksreview or catch up on the session. It captures the key ideas and practical insights shared during this lecture on advanced prompting and application strategy.

1. What Was Covered 
This session advanced our understanding of prompt engineering and introduced the strategic concept of building LLM wrappers to deliver targeted value.


Effective Prompt Structure (Recap & Application):
 We revisited and applied the core elements of a well-structured prompt: Instruction, Context, Input Data, and Output Indicator. The critical role of providing clear and sufficient context to guide LLMs and minimize "hallucinations" (factually incorrect or nonsensical outputs) was a key focus.


In-Context Learning (ICL) in Practice:
 We explored practical ways to use few-shot prompting to “teach” an LLM specific tasks or desired output styles by including examples directly within the prompt. This was shown as a powerful and efficient alternative to model fine-tuning.


Case Study – AI-Powered CRM for 100x Engineers:
 Sid shared an example using the OPT framework:


Defined a scoring rubric for lead qualification.
Used few-shot examples in prompts to train an LLM.
Conceptualized an AI CRM with a FastAPI backend and a Streamlit frontend.


LLM Wrappers as a Viable Business Strategy:
 We examined how building applications that wrap LLM APIs into user-friendly tools or workflows can create strong value. Examples included Perplexity AI and Cursor.


Understanding and Mitigating LLM “Hallucinations”:
 Referencing Andrej Karpathy’s “dream machine” metaphor, we discussed hallucinations as a result of insufficient context. Strategies like In-Context Learning and RAG (to be covered later) were highlighted for mitigation.

2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
The Paramount Importance of Context in Prompting:
 Clear, relevant, and sufficient context dramatically improves LLM output quality.


ICL: A Lean and Agile Approach to Customization:
 Few-shot prompting can yield results comparable to fine-tuning, saving time and resources.


What Exactly is an “LLM Wrapper”?
 An app or service built on top of LLM APIs, adding value through:


Task-specific focus (e.g., summarizing legal docs)


Optimized UI/UX


Integration with external tools/data


Sophisticated prompt engineering


Making LLMs more targeted and useful


A Structured Approach to AI Application Building:


Define the Problem: Use frameworks like OPT.


Plan the Solution: Draft a PRD with clear features and flows.


Design Prompts: Use structured, context-rich prompts.


Build & Test Iteratively: Break down dev into small tested parts.


LLM “Hallucinations” as Guided Creativity:
 They reflect the generative nature of LLMs. It’s our job to provide guardrails—precise instructions, good examples, and solid context.

3. Tools & Frameworks Introduced 
FastAPI:
 Python framework for the backend—handles prompt logic and LLM interaction.


Streamlit:
 Python UI library for quick frontends—used for the conceptual AI CRM interface.


Groq Cloud:
 LLM API provider (e.g., for LLaMA models), powering the AI CRM.


SerpAPI:
 Google Search API, used optionally for gathering contextual info about leads.


Cursor:
 AI-first code editor—great for generating code from PRDs. Uses @<doc_name> (e.g., @SerpAPI) to reference docs for accurate code generation.


OpenAI API / Claude API:
 Examples of powerful LLM APIs that successful wrappers like Perplexity and Cursor are built on.

4. Implementation Insights 
Designing the Lead Qualification Logic (Workflow):


Define a Scoring Rubric:
 Include weighted criteria like technical background, goals, and engagement.


Craft a Comprehensive Few-Shot Prompt:
 Include:
System message defining LLM’s role
The rubric itself
Few-shot examples with ideal outputs
New lead input data
Desired JSON output format (score, reasoning, status)


Backend Integration (Conceptual):
Create a FastAPI endpoint (e.g., /qualify_lead)
Dynamically insert lead data into prompt
Send to LLM (e.g., Grok Cloud)
Parse JSON response and return to frontend


Leveraging Cursor for Efficient Code Generation:
 Use PRDs as input. Cursor’s @<doc_name> feature ensures code is generated using up-to-date API references.

5. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: If LLMs are so powerful, why do I need detailed prompts and context?
 A: LLMs are not mind-readers. They need clear input to generate accurate, relevant output. Vague prompts lead to vague results.


Q: When should I fine-tune an LLM instead of using In-Context Learning?
 A: Start with prompt engineering/ICL. Fine-tune only when:
You need to embed deep domain knowledge.
You want a consistent tone or style.
You have a large dataset for training.
You need faster, cheaper inference at scale.


Q: If I build an “LLM wrapper,” won’t my business be easy to copy?
 A: Only if it adds no unique value. Defensibility comes from:
Superior UI/UX
Sophisticated prompting
Unique integrations or proprietary logic
Focused niche or workflow
Strong brand/community

Define the department's mission.

List the department's core functions.
Example: Ticket management, issue escalation, FAQ handling

Example: Answer FAQS, escalate unresolved issues, collect feedback

User inputs a department-specific query
Chatbot responds based on defined processes and tasks
Bonus: integrate workflow actions like form submission or reminders

Lab Session- AI Filmmaking by Pranay

Objective of the Session

To introduce participants to AI-assisted filmmaking workflows.

To provide clarity on specific AI models and software used in video generation, editing, and storytelling.

To guide students toward building their first AI-powered film prototypes.

Technologies and Tools Discussed

Runway Gen-2 / Gen-3: AI video generation models used for cinematic sequences.

Pika Labs: Tool for short-form AI video generation.

Stable Diffusion XL (SDXL): Diffusion model for creating high-quality frames.

ComfyUI: Node-based UI for building modular video and diffusion workflows.

ChatGPT / other LLMs: For scriptwriting, ideation, and narration prompts.

Descript, Kapwing: AI-based editing and transcription tools for refining outputs.

Cloud GPUs (16–24 GB VRAM recommended): Provided for heavy processing tasks like video rendering and fine-tuning models.

Key Concepts Mentors Emphasized

Script → Visual → Edit pipeline: Beginning with text/script generation using LLMs, producing visuals with Runway/Pika/SDXL, then editing and refining.

Iterative workflow: AI outputs are treated as drafts, refined by human creativity.

No-code vs advanced paths: Beginners can rely on Runway/Pika, while advanced users can leverage ComfyUI and diffusion fine-tuning.

Practical application focus: Tools chosen for immediate usability in projects.

Learning Outcomes

Participants were expected to:

Gain familiarity with key AI video generation platforms (Runway, Pika, SDXL, ComfyUI).

Understand how to structure an AI filmmaking workflow end-to-end.

Apply LLMs to pre-production (scripts, ideas, narration).

Produce their first AI-powered film prototype by combining tools.

Orientation and session setup.

Introduction to AI filmmaking and the no-code tool landscape.

Runway and Pika Labs for fast video generation.

This session provided the technical and conceptual foundation for AI filmmaking. It ensured participants knew the exact technologies to use, how to connect them in a workflow, and how to start moving from ideation to their first AI-generated film.


Lecture 11: Function, Tool Calling and Intro to MCP

This doc is meant to help you folks review or catch up on the session. It captures the key ideas and practical insights shared during this lecture on advanced LLM optimizations.

1. What Was Covered 
Recap of Prompt Engineering & Context:
 We revisited the core principles of prompt engineering, emphasizing the four key parts of a good prompt: Instruction, Context, Input Data, and Output Indicator. A major highlight was the importance of context without which LLMs hallucinate. For example, asking about "100x Engineers tracks" without context leads to hallucination, but with proper context, it leads to accurate answers.


The Problem of Automating Context Filling:
 LLMs often need external, real-time context (e.g., weather or flight data). Manually copying this into prompts is not scalable. This raises the question: how can we automate context fetching?


Function Calling / Tool Calling as a Solution:
 Function calling allows LLMs to interact with external tools:


Define tools (functions) with names, descriptions, and expected parameters (often via JSON schema).
The LLM analyzes the user's request and identifies a suitable tool.
The LLM outputs the tool name and arguments it extracted.
The application executes the tool (e.g., an API call) and optionally sends the result back to the LLM for generating a user-facing response.


Limitations of Function Calling at Scale:
 While powerful, it becomes complex with many tools, requiring authentication, error handling, and efficient orchestration.


Introduction to MCP (Model Context Protocol):
 MCP is a standardized protocol that helps LLMs interact with tools more efficiently.


Analogy: Like HTTP for the internet, MCP introduces a client-server model.


MCP Clients: e.g., Claude Desktop or Cursor IDE.


MCP Servers: e.g., Gmail, Google Drive, or custom APIs exposing functionality via the MCP standard.
Benefits: Standardization, discoverability, and scalable tool integration.


Vision for the Future (LLMO):
 LLMO (Large Language Model Optimization) is similar to SEO, where services optimize their MCP servers to be easily discovered and used by LLMs.

2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
Solving LLM Hallucination with Dynamic Context:
 LLMs can't access real-time or private data by default. Function calling and MCP help them fetch such data when needed.


Function Calling: LLM Identifies, Host Executes:
 The LLM decides which tool to use and what parameters to send. Execution happens in your application.


JSON Schema for Tool Definition:
 Tool definitions follow a structured JSON schema to help the LLM understand the tool and its usage.


3-Layer Architecture:


LLM Layer: Understands the user prompt and outputs function call intent.


API Layer: Executes the function/tool.


LLM Layer (again): Formats the output into a natural language response.


MCP as a Standardization and Scalability Layer:
 MCP reduces developer effort by making tools discoverable and interoperable via a common protocol.


The Shift to Automated Tool Use:
 We’re moving from manual context injection to LLMs autonomously deciding when and how to fetch context via tools.


LLMO – Future Discoverability for AI Tools:
 Just as SEO helps websites rank better on search engines, LLMO will help tools be better discovered and used by LLMs.





3. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: How does the LLM decide when to use a tool instead of answering directly?
 A: It depends on:
The user’s prompt (e.g., asking for current data).
Clarity of the tool descriptions.
System prompt guardrails (e.g., instruct the LLM when to use tools).
LLM API settings like tool_choice: "auto".
The LLM’s internal training.


Q: What if I define many tools? How does the LLM choose the right one?
 A: The LLM uses semantic understanding:
Clear tool descriptions help.
If a request is ambiguous, the LLM may ask for clarification.
For complex apps, use a “router” pattern: first classify the user intent, then narrow the tool options.


Q: What’s the difference between Function Calling and MCP?
 A:
Function Calling is developer-managed; you define and wire up tools manually.
MCP is standardized and ecosystem-driven; tools expose capabilities via a common protocol, making them discoverable and reusable across apps.
