Lecture 12: Function, Tool Calling and MCPs (Practical)

This doc is meant to help you folks review or catch up on the session. It captures the key ideas and practical insights shared during this hands-on lecture on implementing advanced LLM interactions.

1. What Was Covered:
This session was a hands-on dive into the practical implementation of Function Calling (Tool Calling), building directly on the concepts introduced in Lecture 11. The primary goal was to take the 3-layer architecture (LLM Layer → API/Execution Layer → External Tool/API) and bring it to life with Python code.


Recap of Function Calling Architecture: We quickly reviewed the flow: a user prompt goes to an LLM, which identifies a need for an external tool, specifies the tool and its arguments; our application code then executes this tool (e.g., calls an external API) and feeds the result back to the LLM to generate a final, user-friendly response.


Hands-on Python Implementation: The core of the session was a live coding demonstration (using Cursor and Python) to build a weather information system using this architecture:


Tool Definition: We defined a get_current_weather tool using a JSON-like Python dictionary structure, specifying its name, description, and the location parameter it requires. This definition is passed to the LLM.


First LLM Call (Tool Identification & Argument Extraction): We made an API call to Groq Cloud (using a Llama model). The prompt was a user question (e.g., "What's the weather in Bengaluru?"), and we included our tool definition. The LLM's response correctly identified the get_current_weather function and extracted "Bengaluru" as the location argument.


Local Function Execution (API Layer): We wrote a Python function also named get_current_weather that:


Initially, returned mock (static) weather data to test the flow.


Later, was updated to make a real API call to the OpenWeatherMap API using the location argument received from the LLM and an API key (managed via a .env file).


Second LLM Call (Natural Language Response Generation): After getting the actual weather data from OpenWeatherMap, we made a second call to the Groq Cloud LLM. This time, the prompt included the original user question and the fetched weather data (as context). The LLM was instructed to generate a conversational, natural language answer based on this data.


Interactive Exercise for Mentees: You were tasked with extending this implementation:


Complete the Loop: Ensure the weather data fetched from OpenWeatherMap is correctly passed as context to the second LLM call to generate the final user-facing response.


Add a Second Tool: Define and implement another tool (e.g., get_current_humidity or get_current_time) and modify the logic so the LLM can choose between the available tools based on the user's prompt.


Debugging Common Issues: We encountered and discussed common problems like API key errors (e.g., incorrect keys, environment variable issues) and API version mismatches (e.g., using a URL for OpenWeatherMap API v2.5 when the key is for v3.0), emphasizing the need for careful checking of API documentation.


Scaling Function Calls and the Transition to MCP: We briefly touched upon the limitations of managing many individual function calls (complexity, maintenance) and how this naturally leads to the need for more standardized solutions like the Model Context Protocol (MCP).


Further Exploration of MCP: We looked at resources like the "Daily Dose of AI" newsletter and the Latent Space podcast for deeper insights into MCP, and how MCP aims to create an interoperable ecosystem of tools for LLMs using a client-server architecture (e.g., Claude Desktop or Cursor as MCP Clients, and services like Google Drive or Playwright offering MCP Servers).


Live Demo of MCP with Cursor and Playwright: A practical demonstration showed how Cursor (as an MCP Client) could use the Playwright MCP Server to perform browser automation tasks (like searching for weather or testing a website) based on natural language prompts, without the developer writing the direct Playwright code.


2. Key Concepts & Ideas 
Practical Implementation of the 3-Layer Function Calling Architecture: This session was all about seeing the theory from L12 in action. The flow is: User Prompt → LLM (identifies tool & args) → Your Code/API Layer (executes tool, gets data) → LLM (formats data into user response) → User.


The LLM as an "Intent Parser" and "Argument Extractor": In the first LLM call, its primary role is to understand the user's goal from their natural language prompt and, if a relevant tool is defined, to identify which tool to use and to extract the necessary pieces of information (arguments) from the prompt to pass to that tool.


Your Code as the "Execution Engine": The LLM doesn't run the external API calls itself. Your Python script (or other backend code) acts as the execution environment. It receives the LLM's instruction (which function to call and with what arguments) and then performs the actual action (like making the HTTP request to OpenWeatherMap).


The Second LLM Call for Natural Language Formatting: Raw data from an API (often JSON) isn't very user-friendly. A second LLM call is often used to take this raw data, combine it with the original user query, and generate a response that is conversational, easy to understand, and directly answers the user's question.


Importance of Clear Tool Definitions: The quality of the name, description, and parameter definitions you provide for your tools directly impacts how well the LLM can understand when and how to use them. Clear, unambiguous descriptions are key.


Error Handling is Crucial: When dealing with external API calls, things can go wrong (network issues, invalid API keys, API rate limits, unexpected responses). Your execution environment code needs to include robust error handling (e.g., using try-except blocks in Python) to manage these situations gracefully.


System Prompts and Guardrails for Guiding LLM Tool Use: You can use system prompts to give the LLM overall instructions on how it should behave regarding tool usage (e.g., "If you need real-time data, use the available tools. If you don't know the answer and no tool can help, say so clearly."). This helps in managing the LLM's decision-making process.


tool_choice: 'auto' vs. Specific Tool Selection: When making the LLM call, you can often let the LLM automatically decide which tool to use (tool_choice: 'auto') if multiple tools are relevant, or you can sometimes force it to consider a specific tool if your application logic dictates it.


MCP as a More Scalable and Standardized Approach to Tool Use: While function calling is powerful for integrating specific tools you manage, MCP aims to create a broader ecosystem. With MCP, tool providers (like Google for Gmail, or a weather service) would offer standardized MCP "servers." Your LLM application (acting as an MCP "client") could then discover and use these tools through a common protocol, reducing the need for custom integration code for every single tool. This is about making tools more like plug-and-play components for LLMs.


The Power of Combining LLMs with Browser Automation (Playwright MCP Example): The demo with Cursor and Playwright MCP showed how an LLM can be given high-level tasks (like "test this website for broken links" or "find the price of this item on Amazon") and then use a browser automation tool to navigate websites, extract information, and perform actions, all orchestrated via natural language and the MCP.


3. Tools & Frameworks shown
Python: The primary language used for the hands-on implementation of the function calling logic and the API layer.


Groq Cloud API (with Llama models): Used as the Large Language Model service for both identifying the tool/arguments and for generating the final natural language response.


groq (Python library): The SDK for interacting with Groq Cloud.


OpenWeatherMap API: The external third-party API that our Python function called to fetch real-time weather data. An API key was required for this.


Cursor: The AI-first IDE used for writing, running, and debugging the Python code. Also demonstrated as an MCP Client capable of using the Playwright MCP.


JSON: Used for defining the structure of the tools (functions) passed to the LLM (name, description, parameters) and often for parsing the arguments returned by the LLM.


json.loads(): Python function to parse a JSON string into a Python dictionary.


.env files / python-dotenv: Used for securely managing API keys (for Groq Cloud and OpenWeatherMap) by storing them as environment variables separate from the code.


Playwright MCP: An example of an MCP Server that provides browser automation capabilities. It was used in conjunction with Cursor (acting as an MCP Client) to perform web-based tasks.


Claude Desktop Application (by Anthropic): Referenced again as an example of an MCP Client that can integrate with various MCP Servers (like Google Drive, Gmail).


GitHub: The platform where the lecture's example code (100x-llm/function-calling/) is hosted for mentees to access and build upon.

 Python code:

            "description": "Get the current weather in a given location",
            "parameters": {
                "type": "object",
                "properties": {
                    "location": {
                        "type": "string",
                        "description": "The city and state, e.g. San Francisco, CA",

                "required": ["location"],

]

Debugging Tip for API Key Issues:
 If an API call fails with an "invalid API key" error, try hardcoding the key directly in your script temporarily to check if the issue is with environment variable loading or the key itself. Also verify the API endpoint URL and version, as different versions (e.g., OpenWeatherMap v2.5 vs. v3.0) may require different keys or URL formats.


Using Playwright MCP with Cursor (Conceptual Steps):


Install/configure Playwright MCP in Cursor's settings by adding its JSON configuration.


Reload Cursor.


In a Cursor chat (often in Agent Mode), give a natural language instruction like:
 "Use Playwright to go to google.com and search for 'current temperature in Bangalore'."


Cursor (as MCP Client) communicates with the Playwright MCP Server.


Playwright MCP executes browser automation steps (opens browser, navigates, types, extracts info).


The result is returned to Cursor, which then presents it, often summarized by an LLM.





5. Common Mentee Questions 
Q: In the Python code, why do we need to call the get_current_weather function ourselves after the LLM tells us to? Can't the LLM just run it?

 A: The LLM operates in a secure, sandboxed environment and generally cannot directly execute arbitrary code or make external network calls. Instead, it:


Understands your intent from the prompt.


Recognizes a defined "tool" (like get_current_weather) that can help.


Extracts necessary parameters (e.g., location).


Signals back to your application code the function name and arguments it wants to run.


Your application code (the Python script) is responsible for actually executing the function and making the API call. The LLM acts as a smart dispatcher, but your code does the execution.


Q: For the exercise where we add a second tool (e.g., get_current_humidity), how will my Python code know which function (get_current_weather or get_current_humidity) to call after the LLM responds?

 A: The LLM's response includes the function name it wants to invoke in its tool_calls field. Your Python code should inspect this function_name and use conditional logic or a dictionary mapping to call the correct local function. For example:

 Python Code:
# available_functions = {
#     "get_current_weather": your_python_weather_function,
#     "get_current_humidity": your_python_humidity_function
# }
#
# function_name_from_llm = tool_call.function.name
# if function_name_from_llm in available_functions:
#     function_to_execute = available_functions[function_name_from_llm]
#     # ... extract arguments and call function_to_execute ...
# else:
#     print(f"Error: LLM requested unknown function '{function_name_from_llm}'")


This allows dynamic calling of the correct function based on the LLM's decision.


Q: What's the main benefit of using MCP (Model Context Protocol) if I can already do function calling with Python and direct API calls?

 A: While direct function calling gives full control, it can be hard to scale and maintain when integrating many tools or APIs. MCP simplifies this by creating a standardized ecosystem:


Tool Providers Maintain MCP Servers: Instead of you writing custom code for each API (e.g., Google Calendar), providers offer official MCP servers handling API complexities.


Standardized Interaction: Your LLM app (MCP Client) communicates with any MCP Server using the same protocol, no custom code needed per tool.


Discoverability: MCPs can be registered and discovered easily.


Reduced Developer Overhead: Developers focus on orchestrating LLM use rather than API integration details.


Think of function calling as building custom adapters for every device, while MCP is like having a universal standard (USB-C) for plug-and-play compatibility with LLMs.

Lab Session (Combined) - LLM Behind the Scenes by Aishit Dharwal

Objective: Introduce LLM fundamentals and practical retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) engineering so students can map model behavior to implementable pipelines and API integrations.

Techs mentioned (exact names): GPT (ChatGPT / GPT family), Claude, embeddings, RAG, API (LLM APIs).

Process used: generate embeddings from source documents, consider embedding dimensionality and matrix sizing, index vectors (nearest-neighbour retrieval), fetch top-k context, call an LLM (GPT or Claude) via API with retrieved context, and use the model output as the final response — discussed as a simplified, implementable RAG loop.

Mentor focus / key concepts: why GPT behaves the way it does (next-token prediction), practical simplifications for labs, embedding vector size and initialization, where retrieval is needed vs raw in-context prompting, and how to stitch retrieval + API calls into a working pipeline.

Learning outcomes: understand embeddings and their sizing, be able to implement a basic RAG flow (embed → index → retrieve → call LLM API → return), and appreciate trade-offs between model choice (GPT vs Claude) and engineering constraints.

Topics covered (inline): LLM behavior and limits, embeddings and embedding-matrix sizing, RAG architecture and retrieval logic, API integration patterns for LLMs, practical lab simplifications and deployment considerations.

Relevance: prepares students to build and prototype retrieval-backed LLM applications, giving them the exact small-step pipeline and engineering considerations needed to move from theory to a working API-driven prototype.


Lecture 13: Introduction to Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) - Theory
Module: Augmented LLMs
Estimated Duration: 90 minutes
Lesson Type: Theory
_______________________________________________________________________________
Learning Outcomes
By the end of this lesson, you'll be able to:
Understand what RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) is and why it's essential for building AI applications
Identify the three core components of RAG: Retrieval, Augmentation, and Generation
Recognize the limitations of LLMs and how RAG solves the knowledge gap problem
Understand advanced RAG concepts including chunking, embeddings, and vector databases
Differentiate between basic RAG and sophisticated RAG implementations
Know when to use RAG vs simple tool calling approaches
Plan and architect RAG solutions for real-world applications

1. Module Recap: The Journey So Far
The mentor began with a comprehensive recap of the learning journey to help students understand where RAG fits in the bigger picture of AI development.
Key topics covered so far:
Prompt Engineering - Using the instruction, context, input, output framework to design effective prompts
In-Context Learning (Few-Shot Learning) - Showing examples to LLMs so they can learn tasks without fine-tuning
LLM Wrappers - Building Python applications or n8n workflows to give LLMs an execution environment
Tool Calling - Extending LLM capabilities by connecting them to external APIs and services
🔑 Key Philosophy

Each concept builds on the previous one. LLMs alone can only work with their pre-trained knowledge. To make them truly useful, we need to: (1) Give them an execution environment (wrappers), (2) Connect them to external data (tool calling), and (3) Integrate with knowledge bases (RAG).

2. The Core Problem: LLM Knowledge Limitations
The mentor used a practical example to demonstrate a fundamental limitation of LLMs.
Problem Scenario:
When asked "What's the weather in my city?", an LLM cannot provide real-time information because:
LLMs cannot access real-time data or APIs directly
They only have knowledge up to their training cutoff date
They will either refuse to answer or hallucinate (make up) information
This creates a significant gap between what users need and what LLMs can provide
Solution Evolution:
The mentor explained how they previously solved this using LLM wrappers + OpenWeatherMap API to fetch real-time weather data and pass it to the LLM.
💡 Student Insight

This weather API example was the first introduction to the retrieval concept - fetching external data and providing it to the LLM for processing. This is the foundation of RAG.

3. What is RAG? The Three-Component Framework
RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation) is a systematic approach to solving the knowledge limitation problem. It consists of three interconnected processes.
3.1 The Three Components
Component 1: Retrieval
The process of fetching relevant information from external data sources.
Searches through documentation, databases, APIs, or knowledge bases
Identifies and extracts information relevant to the user's query
Acts as the 'memory' or 'knowledge base' for the LLM
Can pull from structured data (databases) or unstructured data (documents, PDFs)

Component 2: Augmentation
The process of combining retrieved information with the user's original query.
Takes the original user query/prompt
Adds the retrieved information as additional context
Creates an 'augmented prompt' that includes both query and relevant data
Essentially fills the context window with relevant information before generation

Component 3: Generation
The LLM generates a response using both the original query and the augmented context.
LLM receives the augmented prompt (query + retrieved data)
Processes all available context to formulate an answer
Generates a response that's grounded in the retrieved information
Output is more accurate and factual because it's based on provided data
🔑 Key Insight

RAG doesn't change how the LLM works internally. Instead, it changes what information the LLM has access to when generating responses. It's like giving the LLM an open-book exam instead of a closed-book exam.

4. RAG Architecture: How It All Flows Together
The mentor explained the complete RAG pipeline flow:
The RAG Lifecycle:
1. User submits a query (e.g., 'Explain our company's return policy')
2. Retrieval System searches the knowledge base (company documentation)
3. Relevant chunks/documents are retrieved (e.g., returns policy section)
4. Augmentation combines user query + retrieved context into single prompt
5. LLM generates response based on augmented prompt
6. User receives accurate answer grounded in company documentation
📊 Real-World Example

Customer Service Bot:
- User: 'Can I return my shoes after 60 days?'
- Retrieval: Fetches return policy section (30-day return window)
- Augmentation: Adds policy to prompt
- Generation: 'According to our return policy, returns are accepted within 30 days of purchase. Unfortunately, 60 days exceeds this window.'

The LLM didn't 'know' the policy - it was retrieved and provided as context.

5. The Knowledge Base Challenge: Why Simple Approaches Fail
The mentor highlighted a critical challenge when building RAG systems: documentation size.
The Problem Scenario:
Imagine you have 300 pages of company documentation and a user asks a specific question. What do you do?
Naive Approach:
Send all 300 pages to the LLM with every query.
Why This Fails:
Context Window Limits: Most LLMs have token limits (e.g., Claude has 200K tokens, but that's still limited)
Cost: Sending massive context with every request multiplies API costs dramatically
Latency: Processing huge contexts takes time, making responses slower
Relevance: The LLM has to process mostly irrelevant information to find what matters
Performance: Including too much irrelevant context can actually degrade answer quality
⚠️ Important

Even with large context windows, you cannot and should not dump entire documentation sets into every query. This is where sophisticated RAG techniques become essential.

6. Advanced RAG: Chunking Strategy
To solve the knowledge base problem, RAG systems use chunking - breaking large documents into smaller, manageable pieces.
What is Chunking?
Chunking is the process of dividing large documents into smaller segments (chunks) that can be individually retrieved and processed.
Why Chunk?
Precision: Retrieve only relevant sections instead of entire documents
Efficiency: Reduce tokens sent to LLM, lowering costs
Performance: Faster retrieval and generation
Context Management: Stay within token limits while handling large knowledge bases
Chunking Strategies:
Fixed-size chunks (e.g., every 500 words)
Semantic chunks (split by paragraphs, sections, or topics)
Overlapping chunks (include some overlap to maintain context)
Hierarchical chunks (summaries at document level, detail at chunk level)
💻 Mentor's Tip

Start with simple fixed-size chunking (e.g., 500-1000 tokens per chunk). Optimize later based on your specific use case and evaluation metrics. Don't over-engineer from the start.

7. Advanced RAG: Embeddings and Vector Representation
Once documents are chunked, the next question is: How do you find relevant chunks quickly?
What are Embeddings?
Embeddings are numerical vector representations of text that capture semantic meaning. Similar concepts have similar embedding vectors.
Key Properties of Embeddings:
Convert text into arrays of numbers (vectors)
Capture semantic meaning, not just keywords
Similar concepts cluster together in vector space
Enable mathematical similarity comparisons
Example: 'king' - 'man' + 'woman' ≈ 'queen'
Why Embeddings Matter for RAG:
Semantic Search: Find chunks based on meaning, not just keyword matching
Speed: Vector similarity is computationally efficient
Accuracy: Can find relevant content even with different wording
Scalability: Can handle millions of chunks efficiently
🔑 Key Concept

Embeddings transform the retrieval problem from 'keyword search' to 'semantic search'. This means if a user asks 'How do I get my money back?' the system can find chunks about 'refund policy' even though the word 'money' doesn't appear in the documentation.

8. Advanced RAG: Vector Databases
Vector databases are specialized storage systems designed to store and search embeddings efficiently.
What is a Vector Database?
A database optimized for storing high-dimensional vectors (embeddings) and performing fast similarity searches.
Popular Vector Databases:
Chroma: Open-source, simple, great for demos and learning
Pinecone: Cloud-based, production-ready, managed service
Weaviate: Open-source, scalable, supports hybrid search
Qdrant: Open-source, high-performance
FAISS: Facebook's library, not a full database but widely used
How Vector Databases Work in RAG:
1. Index Phase: Store all chunk embeddings in the vector database
2. Query Phase: Convert user query to embedding
3. Similarity Search: Find chunks with most similar embeddings
4. Retrieval: Return top K most relevant chunks
5. These chunks then get augmented into the prompt
💡 Student Question

Can we use Pinecone instead of ChromaDB?

Yes! Both provide similar vector search APIs. ChromaDB is simpler for learning and local development. Pinecone is better for production deployments since it's a managed cloud service. The concepts remain the same regardless of which database you choose.

9. Advanced RAG: Similarity Search
Similarity search is the core operation that makes RAG efficient and accurate.
How Similarity Search Works:
1. User Query: 'How do I reset my password?'
2. Query Embedding: Convert query to vector [0.2, 0.8, 0.1, ...]
3. Similarity Calculation: Compare query vector to all chunk vectors
4. Ranking: Rank chunks by similarity score (cosine similarity, dot product)
5. Top K Selection: Return the 3-5 most similar chunks
6. Context Building: These chunks become the retrieval result
Common Similarity Metrics:
Cosine Similarity: Measures angle between vectors (most common)
Euclidean Distance: Measures straight-line distance between vectors
Dot Product: Measures vector alignment
🔑 Key Insight

Similarity search solves the 'needle in haystack' problem. Instead of reading 300 pages, the system mathematically identifies the 3-5 most relevant chunks in milliseconds. This is what makes RAG scalable.

10. The Complete Advanced RAG Pipeline
The mentor synthesized all concepts into a complete advanced RAG architecture:
Indexing Phase (Done Once):
1. Load documentation/data sources
2. Split into chunks using chunking strategy
3. Generate embeddings for each chunk
4. Store embeddings in vector database

Query Phase (For Every User Request):
1. User submits query
2. Convert query to embedding
3. Perform similarity search in vector database
4. Retrieve top K relevant chunks
5. Augment: Combine chunks + query into prompt
6. Generate: LLM produces response based on augmented prompt
7. Return response to user
📊 Architecture Summary

Advanced RAG = Chunking + Embeddings + Vector DB + Similarity Search + LLM

This architecture allows you to:
- Handle unlimited documentation size
- Retrieve only relevant information
- Stay within token limits
- Reduce costs
- Improve accuracy
- Scale to production

11. RAG vs Tool Calling: Understanding the Overlap
The mentor addressed a common point of confusion: the overlap between RAG and tool calling.
The Overlap:
Both involve retrieving external information
Both augment the LLM's capabilities
Both can use similar implementation patterns (API calls, data fetching)
The weather API example demonstrated both concepts
The Key Differences:
Tool Calling:
Focus: Executing actions or fetching real-time data
Example: Call weather API, send email, book appointment
Data: Usually structured, API responses
Retrieval: Often simple, single-source
When: Need to perform actions or get current data

RAG:
Focus: Knowledge retrieval from large document collections
Example: Search company docs, retrieve policy sections
Data: Usually unstructured (PDFs, docs, text)
Retrieval: Complex, semantic search across many documents
When: Need to find relevant info from large knowledge bases
Techniques: Chunking, embeddings, vector databases
💡 When to Use Which?

Simple Tool Call: If you're just calling one API and the data fits in the context window, tool calling is sufficient.

Basic RAG: If you need to search through a moderate amount of documentation.

Advanced RAG: When documentation exceeds context window limits, or when you need sophisticated semantic search capabilities.

Many production systems use BOTH - tool calling for actions and API data, RAG for knowledge retrieval.

12. Best Practices and Implementation Strategy
The mentor provided practical guidance on how to approach RAG implementation.
Start Simple, Then Optimize:
1. Level 1: Basic Context Filling - Just stuff docs into context window if they fit
2. Level 2: Simple Chunking - Split docs and retrieve relevant chunks
3. Level 3: Semantic Search - Add embeddings and vector database
4. Level 4: Advanced Techniques - Implement re-ranking, hybrid search, etc.
Evaluation Strategy:
Don't need fancy evaluation initially
Manually test: Does it give the right answer based on the query?
Check if it's within your budget (token costs)
If yes to both, you're good to go
Only add complexity when you hit limitations
When to Invest in Advanced RAG:
Documentation exceeds context window limits
Need to scale to thousands/millions of documents
Response quality is insufficient with basic approach
Need better precision in retrieval
Cost optimization becomes important at scale
🔑 Key Philosophy

Don't over-engineer from the start. Begin with the simplest approach that solves your problem. Get feedback, evaluate results, and add sophistication only when needed. Many production RAG systems started as simple context stuffing and evolved over time.

13. Key Technical Terms and Concepts
Essential vocabulary from this lecture:
RAG: Retrieval-Augmented Generation - A technique to enhance LLMs with external knowledge
Chunking: Splitting large documents into smaller, manageable segments
Embeddings: Vector representations of text that capture semantic meaning
Vector Database: Specialized database for storing and searching embeddings
Similarity Search: Finding vectors most similar to a query vector
Semantic Search: Search based on meaning rather than keywords
Context Window: The maximum amount of text an LLM can process at once
Augmentation: Combining user query with retrieved information
Indexing: Pre-processing and storing documents for efficient retrieval
Cosine Similarity: Metric for measuring similarity between vectors
Top K: The K most relevant chunks retrieved for a query

15. Student Q&A Highlights
Question: Where do I register for the hackathon?
Answer: No registration needed. All Discord members are automatically invited. Details and timing will be shared after this lecture.

Question: Can you share RAG resources?
Answer: Resources will be shared tonight and after tomorrow's lecture. Starting with foundational understanding is important before diving into advanced resources.

Question: Why not use Small Language Models (SLMs) instead of LLMs?
Answer: SLMs are great for specific, narrow tasks (e.g., summarization, simple Q&A). However, they lack capabilities that LLMs have out-of-the-box like tool calling, general reasoning, and broad domain knowledge. To make SLMs perform comparable tasks, you'd need to fine-tune them extensively. Use case matters - for focused tasks with limited scope, SLMs are excellent and cost-effective. For general-purpose applications requiring complex reasoning and tool use, LLMs are better suited.
📊 SLM vs LLM Trade-offs

SLMs: Better for specific tasks, lower compute, faster, cheaper. BUT: require fine-tuning for new tasks, limited tool calling, less general knowledge.

LLMs: Better for general tasks, robust tool calling, extensive knowledge. BUT: higher cost, more compute, slower.

Choose based on your specific requirements, not trends.

Lecture 14: Building RAG Apps with Three Levels (Practical)
Module: Augmented LLMs
Lesson Type: Practical/Hands-on Implementation
Focus: Building production-grade RAG with vector embeddings, chunking, and retrieval strategies

Learning Outcomes
By the end of this session, you will be able to:
Understand and solve the hallucination problem in LLMs using RAG
Select appropriate vector embedding models using the MTEB leaderboard
Implement indexing and chunking strategies with metadata for better retrieval
Build three progressive levels of RAG: Naive, Intermediate, and Advanced
Combine keyword search, semantic search, and reranking for optimal results

1. Recap: Hallucinations and the RAG Solution
The session began with a recap of the core problem RAG solves: hallucinations in LLMs. Hallucinations occur because LLMs generate text by pattern prediction, essentially dreaming facts when they lack proper information.
The Core Problem
LLMs hallucinate because they generate based on patterns, not facts
They lack access to real-time or domain-specific data
Without proper context, they confidently provide incorrect information
The RAG Solution
Provide LLMs with detailed, factual documents in the context window
LLMs excel at understanding and answering when given the right information
By connecting to trusted sources, we ensure factually correct responses
🔑 Key Philosophy
LLMs are great at understanding and processing information, but need factual documents. RAG bridges this gap by retrieving the right information before generating responses.
The weather example demonstrated this: connecting the LLM to a real-time weather API enabled accurate, cited responses.
2. Mapping Concepts to Code
The mentor emphasized the importance of mapping conceptual knowledge to actual code, following the bootcamp teaching methodology.
The Learning Framework
Start with first principles and conceptual understanding
Map concepts to practical code implementation
Build foundational knowledge that enables independent development
See how theoretical concepts reflect in actual running programs
💡 Student Question
Many students reported understanding concepts but feeling lost while coding. This is normal and improves with practice through repeated mapping of theory to implementation.
3. Vector Embeddings: The Foundation
Vector embeddings are the core technology enabling RAG. They solve the critical problem of semantic search by converting text into numerical representations.
What Are Vector Embeddings?
Convert textual data into numerical format (vectors)
Represent words/phrases in multidimensional space
Capture semantic meaning, not just exact keyword matches
Enable natural language queries without requiring exact keywords
How Dimensions Work
Embeddings use dimensions to capture different traits or aspects of meaning. More dimensions = more nuanced representation.
Early models: Word2Vec used approximately 200 dimensions
Modern models: 384 to 4096+ dimensions
Each dimension captures one trait or aspect of meaning
Example: "cat" represented as [1.2, 3.4, 0.5, ...] across n dimensions
📊 Real-World Example
The word "Apple" can mean: (1) a fruit, (2) the technology company, (3) a record label. A low-dimensional embedding might confuse these, but a high-dimensional model with 4096 dimensions can capture all different meanings and contexts.
4. Selecting Embedding Models: MTEB Leaderboard
The mentor introduced MTEB (Massive Text Embedding Benchmark) as the standard tool for evaluating and selecting embedding models.
What is MTEB?
Massive Text Embedding Leaderboard hosted on Hugging Face
Benchmarks 100+ text embedding models across 1000+ languages
Built using Gradio, showing rankings and performance metrics
Helps developers choose the right embedding model for their use case
Top Models Mentioned
Llama Embed Nemotron: 8B params, 4096 dimensions (currently ranks #1)
Embedding Gemma: 307M params, 768 dimensions (good balance)
Qwen: 1024 dimensions (ranks #5)
OpenAI text-embedding-ada: Closed source, expensive but effective
Model Selection Strategy
✅ Pro Tip
Start with the smallest, cheapest, fastest model and move up the ladder only if needed. Do not over-engineer from the start.
Start with smaller models (e.g., 384 dimensions, 27M parameters)
Test performance on your specific use case
Move to larger models only if results are insufficient
Balance between accuracy and computational cost
Specialized domains (legal, medical) may need higher-dimensional models
Key insight: Higher dimensions do not always mean better results. It depends on your data and use case. Always judge cost versus benefit.
5. The Indexing Process
Indexing is the process of converting raw, unstructured text data into vector embeddings that can be stored and searched efficiently.
What Happens During Indexing?
Raw text data is processed by the embedding model
Each piece of text is converted into a numerical vector
Vectors are stored in a way that enables fast similarity search
The result is an index - a searchable database of embeddings
Index Structure
An index contains vectors like:
# Example of embedded data (simplified)
"cat" -> [1.2, 3.4, 0.5, 2.1, ...]  # 384 dimensions
"dog" -> [1.5, 3.1, 0.6, 2.3, ...]
"apple" -> [0.2, 1.8, 4.2, 0.9, ...]

# Each word/phrase is represented by n numbers
# where n = number of dimensions in the embedding model
Why Indexing Solves Natural Language Queries
Traditional keyword search requires exact matches
Vector embeddings capture semantic meaning
Similar concepts end up close together in vector space
Query: "canine" will match documents about "dogs" even without the exact word
6. Chunking Strategies
Chunking is the process of breaking down large documents into smaller, manageable pieces. It is essential because: (1) embedding models have token limits, and (2) smaller chunks provide more precise retrieval.
Why Chunking Matters
LLMs have context window limitations
Large documents contain multiple topics
Smaller chunks provide more precise matching
Better relevance when retrieving information
Chunking + Indexing = Complete Solution
First: Create vector embeddings (indexing)
Then: Break documents into chunks based on the index
Each chunk gets metadata for filtering
Result: Fast, accurate retrieval of relevant information
⚠️ Important
Indexing alone will not help. Chunking alone will not help. You need BOTH working together. The index tells you how to organize chunks, and chunks make retrieval precise.
7. Metadata: Enhancing Retrieval
Metadata is additional information attached to each chunk that enables filtering and more precise retrieval.
What is Metadata?
Labels or filters attached to each chunk
Examples: city name, date, category, document source
Enables filtering before semantic search
Dramatically improves retrieval accuracy
Metadata Example: Weather Data
In a weather application:
City: "New York", "London", "Tokyo"
Metric: "temperature", "humidity", "wind speed"
Date: "2024-01-15"
Source: "WeatherAPI", "NOAA"
With metadata, you can filter: "Give me temperature data for New York from January 2024" before doing semantic search on the chunks.
Metadata Quality Depends On Your Data
Structured data: easier to extract metadata
Unstructured data: may require NLP or manual tagging
Domain-specific: legal docs and medical records have different metadata needs
8. The Complete RAG Pipeline
Putting it all together, here is the end-to-end RAG process:
Data Preparation Phase
1. Gather raw documents (PDFs, web pages, databases)
2. Select an embedding model (start small, e.g., 384 dimensions)
3. Index: Convert text to vector embeddings
4. Chunk: Break documents into manageable pieces
5. Add metadata: Attach filters to each chunk
6. Store: Save to vector database (Pinecone, Chroma, etc.)
Query Phase (Retrieval)
1. User asks a question
2. Convert question to vector embedding (using same model)
3. Apply metadata filters if needed
4. Search vector database for similar chunks
5. Retrieve top K most relevant chunks
6. Pass chunks + question to LLM
7. LLM generates answer using retrieved context
🔑 Key Insight
The embedding model used for indexing MUST be the same model used for query embedding. Otherwise, the vectors will not align and search will not work.
9. Three Levels of RAG Architecture
The mentor introduced a framework for building RAG systems at three levels of sophistication.
Level 1: Naive RAG
The simplest implementation that works for basic use cases.
Uses semantic search only
Embed query → search vector database → retrieve chunks
No preprocessing, no reranking
Fast but can miss relevant results
When to use: Prototyping, small datasets, proof-of-concept projects
Level 2: Intermediate RAG
Adds keyword search and metadata filtering to improve recall.
Combines semantic search + keyword search
Hybrid approach catches more relevant documents
Keyword search finds exact matches (e.g., product codes, IDs)
Semantic search finds conceptually similar content
Use metadata filters to narrow search space
When to use: Production systems with diverse query types, medium-sized datasets
Level 3: Advanced RAG
Production-grade system with reranking and query optimization.
Hybrid search (semantic + keyword)
Reranking: Use a specialized model to reorder results
Query expansion: Generate multiple variations of user query
Result fusion: Intelligently merge results from multiple searches
HyDE: Generate ideal answer, embed it, search for similar docs
When to use: High-stakes applications, large datasets, quality-critical use cases
✅ Implementation Advice
Do not start with Level 3. Build Level 1, validate it works, measure performance, then incrementally add complexity only if needed. Over-engineering wastes time and resources.
10. Keyword vs Semantic vs Hybrid Search
Keyword Search (Traditional)
Exact text matching (BM25, TF-IDF algorithms)
Fast and precise for specific terms
Example: searching for "iPhone 15 Pro Max" finds exact product
Limitation: misses synonyms and related concepts
Semantic Search (Vector-Based)
Searches by meaning, not exact words
Uses vector embeddings to find similar concepts
Example: "affordable smartphone" matches docs about "budget phones"
Limitation: can miss exact matches or specific IDs
Hybrid Search (Best of Both)
Runs keyword and semantic search in parallel
Combines results using score fusion
Catches both exact matches and conceptually similar documents
Industry standard for production RAG systems
Implementation note: Most vector databases (Pinecone, Weaviate) have built-in hybrid search. You do not need to implement the fusion algorithm yourself.
11. Reranking: The Final Step
Reranking is the process of taking initial search results and reordering them using a more sophisticated model.
Why Rerank?
Initial retrieval casts a wide net (returns 50-100 documents)
Not all results are equally relevant
Reranking model scores each document against the query
Final output: top 5-10 most relevant documents
How Reranking Works
1. Retrieve K documents (e.g., 50) using hybrid search
2. Pass query + each document to reranking model
3. Model outputs relevance score for each pair
4. Sort by score, keep top N (e.g., 5)
5. These N documents go to the LLM as context
Popular Reranking Models
Cohere Rerank API
Cross-encoder models from Hugging Face
Custom fine-tuned rerankers for specific domains
💡 Performance Tip
Reranking is computationally expensive. Only use it in Level 3 RAG when precision is critical. For most applications, Level 2 (hybrid search) is sufficient.
12. Practical Implementation Tips
Choosing Your Tech Stack
Embedding model: Start with small open-source models (384 dims)
Vector database: Chroma (local), Pinecone (cloud), Weaviate (self-hosted)
Framework: LangChain mentioned but not required
LLM: Any model (GPT-4, Claude, open-source Llama)
Development Workflow
1. Start with a small test dataset (100 documents)
2. Build Level 1 Naive RAG, verify it works end-to-end
3. Measure: precision, recall, latency
4. If quality is insufficient, move to Level 2
5. Only add reranking (Level 3) if absolutely necessary
Common Pitfalls to Avoid
Using different embedding models for indexing vs querying
Chunks that are too large (>1000 tokens) or too small (<50 tokens)
Forgetting to add metadata for filtering
Over-engineering with Level 3 before validating Level 1 works
Not benchmarking embedding models on your specific data
🔑 Success Criteria
A good RAG system: (1) Returns relevant documents >90% of the time, (2) Responds in <2 seconds, (3) Costs are sustainable for your use case, (4) Users trust the sources it cites.
13. Hackathon Preparation
The mentor emphasized using the learned concepts for the hackathon, providing code snippets and boilerplate for implementation.
What Was Provided
Boilerplate for RAG pipeline setup
Example embedding and indexing code
Vector database connection examples
Retrieval and generation pipeline
Hackathon Success Tips
Use the provided boilerplate as a starting point
Focus on one specific use case (do not try to be too general)
Build Level 1 RAG first, then iterate if time allows
Test with real queries from your target users
Document your design decisions (which embedding model, why?)
The mentor encouraged participants to apply their learning and build real applications with the knowledge gained.
Key Takeaways
RAG solves hallucinations by providing LLMs with factual, retrieved context
Vector embeddings enable semantic search beyond exact keyword matching
Use MTEB leaderboard to select embedding models; start small and scale up
Indexing + Chunking + Metadata = complete data preparation pipeline
Build incrementally: Level 1 → Level 2 → Level 3 as needed
Hybrid search (keyword + semantic) is the production standard
Reranking is powerful but expensive; use only when precision is critical

Next Steps: Apply these concepts in the hackathon. Build a Level 1 Naive RAG system first, validate it works, then enhance. Focus on real-world use cases that solve actual problems.

By the end of this lesson, you'll be able to:
Understand and implement metadata filtering to improve RAG precision
Apply hybrid search combining vector and keyword-based retrieval
Utilize query routing and classification for intelligent RAG systems
Implement knowledge graphs for complex relationship representation
Design adaptive RAG architectures for production applications

1. Introduction and Course Context 
The mentor began by explaining the schedule changes due to travel and introduced the focus for the final sessions. Advanced RAG techniques are positioned as critical building blocks for production-ready applications.
Key Points:
This session and the next (memory systems) are the last lectures before guest speaker sessions
Guest speakers include industry practitioners from Amazon Prime, Netflix working on production systems
Today's focus: Advanced RAG techniques to solve real-world retrieval problems
Tomorrow's focus: Memory systems for conversational AI
These concepts are considered the most challenging parts of the module

💡 Learning Strategy

The mentor recommends implementing basic RAG first this weekend, then applying advanced techniques. Avoid feeling overwhelmed by taking notes and planning to revisit the material through Q&A sessions.
2. RAG Fundamentals Recap 
Before diving into advanced techniques, the mentor reviewed the fundamental RAG architecture covered in previous sessions. This serves as the foundation for understanding the improvements.
Key Points:
Basic RAG pipeline: User query → Vectorization → Vector search → Chunk retrieval → Context augmentation → LLM generation
Key components: Embedding models (transformers), vector databases (ChromaDB, Pinecone, Superbase), LLM for generation
MTEB leaderboard remains relevant for choosing embedding models
Recent update: Voyage AI's voyage-3 and voyage-3-lite models (October 2024) show strong performance
Critical concept: RAG is about giving the LLM relevant context from your knowledge base

🔑 Key Insight

RAG creates an index (vector embeddings) during ingestion, then performs semantic search during query time. The quality of your embeddings directly impacts retrieval accuracy.
📊 Embedding Model Update

The mentor highlighted voyage-3 and voyage-3-lite from Voyage AI (released October 2024) as strong options on the MTEB leaderboard. These models show improved performance over previous generations for semantic search tasks.
3. Problems with Basic RAG
The mentor identified three critical problems that basic RAG implementations face in production environments. These problems motivate the need for advanced techniques.
Key Points:
Problem 1: Lack of precision - retrieving too many irrelevant chunks alongside relevant ones
Problem 2: Missing context - semantic search alone misses exact keyword matches (e.g., specific product names, IDs)
Problem 3: Complex relationships - inability to represent multi-dimensional data and entity relationships
Basic vector search returns semantically similar chunks but cannot apply business logic or constraints
Real-world example: Tourism app unable to filter by state when querying for monuments in Karnataka

⚠️ Production Reality

Students reported that basic RAG works for demos but fails in production when precision matters. The mentor emphasized that advanced techniques solve these real-world problems.
4. Advanced Technique 1: Metadata Filtering 
Metadata filtering is the first advanced technique that allows RAG systems to combine semantic search with structured filtering criteria. This dramatically improves precision by applying business logic during retrieval.
Key Points:
Metadata = structured attributes attached to each chunk during ingestion (e.g., state, city, category, date)
Enables filtering before or during vector search (depends on vector database capabilities)
Example: "Show monuments in Karnataka" can filter state="Karnataka" before retrieving chunks
Reduces false positives by constraining search space to relevant data partitions
Most vector databases support metadata filtering (Superbase, Pinecone, Weaviate, Qdrant)

🔑 Key Philosophy

Metadata helps your LLM pick the right amount of information by applying certain constraints or filters on top of semantic search. It bridges the gap between semantic understanding and business logic.
📊 Tourism App Use Case

For a tourism database with monuments across India, basic RAG might return monuments from multiple states when asking about Karnataka. With metadata filtering on state="Karnataka", only relevant monuments are retrieved.
💡 Student Question: What counts as metadata?

Metadata should be structured, filterable attributes like categories, locations, dates, IDs, or tags. Unstructured text should stay in the chunk content. Good metadata design requires understanding your query patterns.
# Example: Metadata structure for tourism database
chunk_metadata = {
    "state": "Karnataka",
    "city": "Mysore",
    "category": "Historical Monument",
    "year_built": 1897,
    "monument_type": "Palace"
}

# Vector database stores:
# 1. Chunk text (vectorized)
# 2. Metadata as structured fields
# 3. Original text for context

# Query with metadata filter:
query = "famous palaces"
metadata_filter = {"state": "Karnataka", "monument_type": "Palace"}
results = vector_db.search(query, filter=metadata_filter)
5. Metadata Filtering Architecture
The mentor presented a detailed architecture diagram showing how metadata filtering integrates into the RAG pipeline. This architecture modifies both the ingestion and query phases.
Key Points:
Ingestion phase adds metadata extraction step: Clean → Normalize → Chunk → Extract Metadata → Create Index
Metadata extraction can be manual (structured data) or LLM-powered (unstructured data)
Query phase adds intent classification: User Query → Classify Intent → Extract Filters → Apply to Vector Search
Intent classification determines which metadata filters to apply (e.g., location, category, date range)
Metadata filters are applied at the vector database level before retrieving chunks
Result: Higher precision retrieval with fewer irrelevant results

✅ Implementation Approach

Use an LLM to classify user intent and extract relevant filter criteria from the query. For example, "monuments in Karnataka built after 1800" should extract state="Karnataka" and year_built > 1800.
📊 Intent Classification Example

Query: "What are some historical sites in Bangalore?" → Intent: tourism query → Filters: state="Karnataka", city="Bangalore", category="Historical"
# Pseudocode for intent classification and metadata extraction

def classify_and_extract_filters(user_query, llm):
    prompt = f"""
    Analyze this query and extract filter criteria:
    Query: {user_query}
    
    Return JSON with:
    - intent: query type (tourism, product, document, etc.)
    - filters: dict of metadata filters to apply
    """
    
    response = llm.invoke(prompt)
    return response['intent'], response['filters']

# Example usage:
query = "monuments in Karnataka"
intent, filters = classify_and_extract_filters(query, llm)
# intent = "tourism"
# filters = {"state": "Karnataka", "category": "Monument"}

# Apply to vector search:
results = vector_db.search(
    query=query,
    filters=filters,
    top_k=5
)
6. Advanced Technique 2: Hybrid Search 
Hybrid search combines vector-based semantic search with keyword-based (BM25/TF-IDF) search to overcome limitations of pure semantic approaches. This technique is essential when exact keyword matches matter.
Key Points:
Problem: Vector search misses exact keyword matches (product IDs, specific names, technical terms)
Solution: Combine semantic search (vectors) with lexical search (keywords)
Hybrid search = Semantic Search + Keyword Search, with weighted combination of results
BM25 algorithm commonly used for keyword matching (similar to traditional search engines)
Results from both searches are merged and re-ranked based on relevance scores
Particularly useful for e-commerce, documentation, and technical content

🔑 Key Insight

Semantic search understands meaning but can miss exact matches. Keyword search finds exact matches but misses semantic relationships. Hybrid search gets the best of both worlds.
📊 E-commerce Example

Query: "iPhone 15 Pro Max" - Semantic search might return iPhone 14 or similar products. Keyword search ensures exact model match. Hybrid search returns the specific product plus semantically similar items.
💡 Student Question: When to use hybrid vs pure vector?

Use hybrid search when: 1) Exact keywords matter (product names, codes), 2) Technical documentation with specific terms, 3) Legal/compliance documents. Use pure vector when: 1) Conceptual queries, 2) Questions requiring semantic understanding, 3) Paraphrased content.
# Hybrid search conceptual implementation

def hybrid_search(query, vector_db, keyword_index, alpha=0.5):
    """
    Combine vector and keyword search results
    alpha: weight between vector (1.0) and keyword (0.0)
    """
    # Perform vector search
    vector_results = vector_db.similarity_search(query, k=10)
    
    # Perform keyword search (BM25)
    keyword_results = keyword_index.search(query, k=10)
    
    # Merge and re-rank results
    combined_results = []
    for doc in vector_results:
        score = alpha * doc.vector_score
        combined_results.append((doc, score))
    
    for doc in keyword_results:
        score = (1 - alpha) * doc.keyword_score
        # Add to existing or create new entry
        existing = [r for r in combined_results if r[0].id == doc.id]
        if existing:
            existing[0] = (doc, existing[0][1] + score)
        else:
            combined_results.append((doc, score))
    
    # Sort by combined score
    combined_results.sort(key=lambda x: x[1], reverse=True)
    return [doc for doc, score in combined_results[:5]]
7. Hybrid Search Architecture 
The mentor explained the architecture for implementing hybrid search, showing how it extends the basic RAG pipeline with parallel search paths that are merged during retrieval.
Key Points:
Ingestion: Store both vector embeddings AND keyword indices (inverted index) for each chunk
Query time: Run vector search and keyword search in parallel
Hybrid vector stores like Weaviate support this natively with automatic index management
Results from both searches are combined using a weighted formula (alpha parameter)
Final reranking step (optional) can further improve result quality
Superbase and Weaviate mentioned as databases with built-in hybrid search support

✅ Implementation Tip

Start with alpha=0.5 (equal weight to vector and keyword). Tune based on your use case: higher alpha (0.7-0.8) for semantic queries, lower alpha (0.3-0.4) for exact match needs.
📊 Weaviate Hybrid Search

Weaviate handles hybrid search automatically. You just specify the alpha parameter and it manages both vector and keyword indices behind the scenes, merging results efficiently.
# Example: Weaviate hybrid search
import weaviate

client = weaviate.Client("http://localhost:8080")

# Hybrid search query
result = client.query.get(
    "Article",
    ["title", "content", "author"]
).with_hybrid(
    query="machine learning applications",
    alpha=0.5,  # 0=pure keyword, 1=pure vector
    top_k=5
).do()

# Access results
articles = result["data"]["Get"]["Article"]
for article in articles:
    print(f"Title: {article['title']}")
    print(f"Author: {article['author']}")
8. Query Routing and Classification 
Query routing adds an intelligence layer that classifies user intent and routes queries to appropriate RAG architectures or data sources. This enables building multi-strategy RAG systems.
Key Points:
Intent classification determines what the user wants to achieve (not just what they asked)
Routes queries to different backends: simple queries → basic RAG, complex queries → advanced RAG
Can route to different data sources based on intent (SQL database vs vector store vs knowledge graph)
Examples: Weather queries → API call, Document queries → Vector search, Calculation queries → Tool use
Enables cost optimization by using simpler models for simple queries
OpenAI's o1 likely uses this approach to route between GPT-4 and GPT-4-turbo based on complexity

🔑 Key Philosophy

Understanding user intent allows you to plan the optimal retrieval strategy. Different questions require different approaches - one size does not fit all in production RAG systems.
📊 GPT-4 Routing Example

The mentor speculates that GPT-4 uses routing: Simple queries → GPT-3.5-turbo, Complex reasoning → GPT-4, Creative tasks → Specialized model. Users never see this routing but get optimal responses.
💡 Student Question: How to train intent classifier?

You need training data: past queries + correct intent labels. Open source datasets exist for common use cases. For custom domains, manually label 100-200 examples and fine-tune a small model like distilBERT.
# Query routing architecture

def route_query(user_query, intent_classifier):
    """
    Route query to appropriate backend based on intent
    """
    # Classify intent
    intent = intent_classifier.predict(user_query)
    
    if intent == "weather":
        # Route to weather API
        return call_weather_api(user_query)
    
    elif intent == "document_search":
        # Route to vector RAG
        return vector_rag_search(user_query)
    
    elif intent == "calculation":
        # Route to math tool
        return calculator_tool(user_query)
    
    elif intent == "complex_reasoning":
        # Route to advanced RAG with knowledge graph
        return knowledge_graph_rag(user_query)
    
    else:
        # Default: basic RAG
        return basic_rag_search(user_query)

# Example usage:
query = "What's the weather in Bangalore?"
response = route_query(query, intent_classifier)
# Routes to weather API, not RAG system
9. Advanced Technique 3: Knowledge Graphs
Knowledge graphs represent information as entities (nodes) and relationships (edges), enabling complex multi-hop reasoning that vector embeddings cannot handle. Google pioneered this approach in 2012 for search.
Key Points:
Knowledge graph = Graph database representing entities and their relationships
Entities are nodes (e.g., Person, Place, Product), relationships are edges (e.g., "works at", "located in")
Enables multi-hop queries: "Show me companies founded by Stanford alumni in AI"
Google shifted from "strings to things" - understanding entities not just text matching
Graph databases use languages like Cypher (Neo4j) or SPARQL for querying relationships
Better than SQL for representing complex, multi-dimensional relationships
LLMs can extract entities and relationships from unstructured text to build graphs

🔑 Key Insight - Strings to Things

Google's 2012 knowledge graph announcement introduced "searching for things not strings." Instead of matching text, understand entities and their relationships to provide direct, accurate answers.
📊 Google Search Example

Search "Taj Mahal" and Google shows a knowledge panel with: location (Agra), built date (1632), architect (Ustad Ahmad Lahauri), UNESCO status - all from the knowledge graph, not webpage text.
📊 Real-World Application

Student built insurance risk assessment system using knowledge graphs. It connects: Patient → Diagnosis → Medications → Risk Factors → Insurance Claims. Enables complex reasoning about risk patterns across 800 million users.
# Knowledge graph structure (conceptual)

# Nodes (Entities)
entities = {
    "taj_mahal": {
        "type": "Monument",
        "name": "Taj Mahal",
        "built_year": 1632
    },
    "agra": {
        "type": "City",
        "name": "Agra",
        "state": "Uttar Pradesh"
    },
    "shah_jahan": {
        "type": "Person",
        "name": "Shah Jahan",
        "role": "Mughal Emperor"
    }
}

# Edges (Relationships)
relationships = [
    ("taj_mahal", "located_in", "agra"),
    ("taj_mahal", "built_by", "shah_jahan"),
    ("taj_mahal", "is_a", "UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site"),
    ("agra", "has_monument", "taj_mahal")
]

# Query examples (Cypher-like):
# "Show monuments in Agra"
# MATCH (m:Monument)-[:located_in]->(c:City {name: 'Agra'})
# RETURN m.name

# "Who built monuments that are UNESCO sites?"
# MATCH (p:Person)<-[:built_by]-(m:Monument)-[:is_a]->(u:UNESCO)
# RETURN p.name, m.name
10. Knowledge Graph + RAG Architecture
The mentor presented a hybrid architecture combining traditional vector RAG with knowledge graphs. This approach layers knowledge graph queries on top of vector search for enhanced reasoning.
Key Points:
Not a replacement - adds knowledge graph as an additional layer to existing RAG
Ingestion: Extract entities and relationships using LLM, store in graph database alongside vector store
Query time: First retrieve relevant chunks from vector store, then query knowledge graph for relationships
Knowledge graph helps with: entity disambiguation, relationship discovery, multi-hop reasoning
Example: Vector search finds monuments, knowledge graph connects related historical events and people
Hybrid approach balances semantic search (vectors) with structured reasoning (graphs)
Popular graph databases: Neo4j, Amazon Neptune, Azure Cosmos DB Graph API

✅ Implementation Strategy

Start with basic RAG, add metadata filtering, then introduce knowledge graphs for specific use cases requiring relationship reasoning. Don't build knowledge graphs unless you need multi-hop queries.
📊 Travel Planning Use Case

Vector search: Find relevant travel destinations. Knowledge graph: Connect destinations → activities → restaurants → hotels → transportation. Enables "plan a 3-day trip in Karnataka visiting historical sites with vegetarian restaurants nearby."
# Hybrid RAG + Knowledge Graph pipeline

def hybrid_kg_rag(user_query, vector_store, knowledge_graph, llm):
    """
    Combine vector RAG with knowledge graph reasoning
    """
    # Step 1: Vector search for relevant content
    relevant_chunks = vector_store.similarity_search(user_query, k=5)
    
    # Step 2: Extract entities from query
    entities = llm.extract_entities(user_query)
    
    # Step 3: Query knowledge graph for relationships
    kg_results = knowledge_graph.query_relationships(entities)
    
    # Step 4: Combine vector context with KG relationships
    combined_context = {
        "chunks": relevant_chunks,
        "entities": entities,
        "relationships": kg_results
    }
    
    # Step 5: Generate response with enriched context
    response = llm.generate(
        query=user_query,
        context=combined_context
    )
    
    return response

# Example usage:
query = "Who built monuments in Karnataka?"
result = hybrid_kg_rag(query, vector_store, kg, llm)
# Vector store: Finds documents about Karnataka monuments
# Knowledge graph: Connects monuments → builders → time period
# LLM: Synthesizes comprehensive answer with relationships
11. Knowledge Graph vs Vector Embeddings
Students asked about the conceptual differences between knowledge graphs and vector embeddings. The mentor clarified that they serve different purposes and solve different problems.
Key Points:
Vector embeddings: Capture semantic similarity in high-dimensional space (768-1536 dimensions)
Knowledge graphs: Represent discrete entities and explicit relationships (deterministic)
Vectors are continuous/fuzzy, graphs are discrete/structured
Vectors better for: semantic search, similarity matching, fuzzy queries
Graphs better for: multi-hop reasoning, explicit relationships, complex queries
Knowledge graphs are closer to SQL (structured) than to vector search (semantic)
Can represent more than 2 dimensions of data - SQL is limited to 2D tables
Both can complement each other in hybrid architectures

🔑 Key Distinction

Knowledge graphs are the middle ground between SQL (pure structured) and vector embeddings (pure semantic). They offer structured relationships with flexibility beyond 2D tables.
💡 Student Question: When to use which?

Use vectors for "find similar" queries. Use graphs for "how are things connected" queries. Use both when you need semantic understanding AND relationship reasoning.
📊 Comparison Example

Query: "AI companies founded by Google employees" - Vector search: Finds companies and people by semantic similarity. Knowledge graph: Traverses Person → worked_at → Google, Person → founded → Company to find exact matches.
12. Practical Guidance and Next Steps
The mentor concluded with practical advice for implementing these techniques and announced an upcoming office hours session for architecture review.
Key Points:
Start with basic RAG implementation this weekend
Add one advanced technique at a time based on your use case needs
Office hours planned in 2 weeks: Present your RAG architecture for feedback
Community-based learning: Share implementations and learn from each other
Tomorrow's session: Memory systems for conversational AI
Focus on understanding use cases, not just implementing every technique
Tune parameters like alpha (hybrid search weight) based on your data and queries

💡 Office Hours Challenge

In 2 weeks: 1) Define your use case, 2) Select appropriate architecture(s), 3) Design sequence diagram showing your RAG flow, 4) Present for community feedback. This hands-on practice will solidify understanding.
📊 Architecture Selection Guide

Basic queries (documents, articles) → Metadata filtering. Exact matches needed (products, codes) → Hybrid search. Complex relationships (social networks, org charts) → Knowledge graphs. Cost optimization → Query routing.


Lecture 16: Memory RAG Techniques
Module: LLM 
 Estimated Duration: 2 hours
 Lesson Type: Theory + Practical Workshop
Learning Outcomes
By the end of this lesson, you'll be able to:

Understand the fundamentals of memory systems in LLM applications
Differentiate between various types of memory (semantic, episodic, procedural, working)
Implement memory layers in RAG applications
Apply memory concepts to build personalized AI agents
Use tools like SuperMemory to manage long-term context
Create Claude Skills that leverage memory patterns


1. Introduction to Memory in LLMs [00:10:00]
Memory in LLM applications refers to the system's ability to retain and recall information from past interactions to provide more personalized and contextually relevant responses.

Key Concepts:

LLMs are stateless by default - they don't remember previous conversations
Memory systems add persistence across interactions
Memory enables personalization and learning from user behavior
Different types of memory serve different purposes

Why Memory Matters:

Improves user experience through personalization
Reduces repetitive explanations
Enables long-term context retention
Makes AI assistants feel more "intelligent" and aware

🔑 Key Philosophy

Memory transforms LLMs from one-shot responders into systems that genuinely learn and adapt to users over time, similar to how human memory works.


2. Types of Memory Systems [00:15:00]
The lecture covered four primary types of memory, each serving distinct purposes in AI systems.

Semantic Memory:

Stores factual knowledge and concepts
Contains general information about the world
Example: "Paris is the capital of France"
Accumulates over time as facts are learned
Similar to encyclopedia-style knowledge

Episodic Memory:

Records specific events and experiences
Contains timestamped, contextual information
Example: "User discussed their project deadline last Tuesday"
Captures the narrative of past interactions
Similar to diary entries or personal history

Procedural Memory:

Stores "how-to" knowledge and skills
Contains step-by-step processes
Example: "How to create a slide deck from concepts"
Enables automated workflows
Similar to learned motor skills in humans

Working Memory:

Temporary, short-term context storage
Active information being currently processed
Limited capacity (context window)
Gets cleared or consolidated into long-term memory
Similar to RAM in computers

How Memory Types Interact:

Working memory pulls from semantic/episodic as needed
Frequently used working memory gets consolidated into semantic memory
Procedural memory automates repeated patterns
All types work together for comprehensive context

💡 Practical Application

In ChatGPT, when it remembers your name and role, that's semantic memory. When it recalls you discussed a project yesterday, that's episodic memory. When it learns your preferred writing style, that's procedural memory.


3. Memory Consolidation Process [00:45:00]
Memory consolidation is the process of moving information from temporary working memory into long-term storage, similar to how human brains process information during sleep.

The Consolidation Workflow:

Information enters working memory during conversation
System identifies frequently accessed or important information
Relevant data gets transferred to semantic or episodic memory
Working memory clears to free up context window
Process repeats continuously

Why Consolidation is Necessary:

Context windows are limited (even with 200K tokens)
Not all information is equally important to retain
Reduces noise and improves retrieval efficiency
Mimics human memory optimization

When Consolidation Happens:

After conversations end
When certain patterns are detected
Periodically based on system design
When working memory approaches capacity

⚠️ Important

Without consolidation, the context window would fill up with irrelevant information, making retrieval slower and less accurate. Consolidation is essential for scalable memory systems.


4. Implementing Memory in RAG Applications [01:00:00]
Memory layers can be added to existing RAG applications to enable long-term context and personalization.

Basic Memory RAG Architecture:

User query comes in
System checks memory layer for relevant context
Retrieved memories augment the prompt
LLM generates response with personalized context
New information gets stored in memory

Key Implementation Patterns:

Memory as a separate vector database
Memory retrieval before document retrieval
Combining memory context with document context
Storing conversation metadata alongside content

Memory Storage Strategy:

Identify what information should be remembered
Extract key entities, preferences, facts
Store with appropriate memory type classification
Add timestamps and context markers
Enable efficient retrieval through embeddings

💡 Student Question: How is memory different from just storing conversation history?

Memory is selective and processed. Conversation history stores everything raw. Memory extracts key information, classifies it by type, and optimizes for retrieval. It's the difference between keeping every receipt vs. maintaining a budget spreadsheet.


5. Practical Example: ChatGPT Memory System [01:15:00]
The lecture included a live demonstration of reverse-engineering ChatGPT's memory system to understand how commercial AI assistants implement memory.

ChatGPT Memory Observations:

Stores user preferences and personal details
Remembers professional context (role, company)
Tracks ongoing projects and goals
Learns communication style preferences
Updates memory based on corrections

Memory Extraction Exercise:

Export ChatGPT memory data
Analyze what types of information are stored
Understand the memory structure
Observe how it influences responses
Identify patterns in what gets remembered

Key Findings:

Memory entries are concise, fact-based
Each entry typically 1-2 sentences
Covers diverse aspects: work, preferences, relationships
Gets updated rather than duplicated
Powers more personalized responses

📊 Real-World Example

When the instructor checked their ChatGPT memory, it contained details about their role at 100xEngineers, preference for technical explanations, and references to past projects like curriculum design. This context was automatically applied in subsequent conversations.


6. SuperMemory Tool Deep Dive [01:45:00]
SuperMemory is an open-source memory management system that can be integrated with LLM applications to provide persistent, queryable memory.

What SuperMemory Provides:

Cross-platform memory storage
Integration with various apps via MCPs
Automatic memory classification (semantic/episodic/procedural)
Memory visualization and management
Fast retrieval and search
Memory consolidation and forgetting mechanisms

Key Features:

43 memories, 10 documents, 55 connections (example stats)
Visualizes memory networks
Shows latest and forgotten memories
Consolidates working memory over time
Forgets unused information to optimize storage

Integration Methods:

MCP (Model Context Protocol) connections
Direct API access
Client libraries for different platforms
Web interface for visualization

How It Works:

Observes user activity across connected apps
Extracts relevant information automatically
Classifies memory by type
Stores with embeddings for retrieval
Surfaces relevant memories during conversations

✅ Pro Tip

SuperMemory claims to be the fastest memory system currently available, with better tool integration than alternatives like MemZero. Consider evaluating both based on your specific use case.


7. Building Claude Skills with Memory Patterns [02:00:00]
The lecture demonstrated how to create Claude Skills that leverage memory and procedural knowledge to automate workflows.

The Skill Creation Process:

Identify a repetitive task (procedural memory)
Define clear input and output
Write instruction prompt
Provide examples of desired output
Configure tools and integrations
Test and refine

Example: Slide Deck Generation Skill:

Input: Concept or topic (e.g., "LLM Memory")
Output: Formatted slide deck
Tools: Google Slides integration
Memory: Uses procedural knowledge of deck structure

Connecting Memory Concepts:

Skills represent procedural memory
They codify "how to do something"
Can pull from semantic memory (facts)
Use episodic memory (past examples)
Automate repeated patterns

Implementation Architecture:

Input (Concept) → Claude Skill (Instructions) → Tools (Google Slides) → Output (Deck)

Key Components:

System prompt with instructions
Output format specification
Tool access configuration
Brand guidelines and assets
Example outputs for reference

🔑 Key Insight

The slide deck skill demonstrated in the lecture was built using the same memory principles taught in the session - it extracted procedural knowledge about deck creation and automated it.

10. Integration with MCP (Model Context Protocol) [02:30:00]
Memory systems can integrate with various applications through MCP to gather context from multiple sources.

What MCP Enables:

Cross-application memory sharing
Unified context from multiple tools
Automatic context gathering
Seamless integration with AI assistants

Example Integrations:

Slack conversations
Google Drive documents
Email content
Browser history
Calendar events

How Memory Uses MCP Data:

Observes user activity across platforms
Extracts relevant information
Builds comprehensive user profile
Surfaces context when needed

Benefits:

No manual memory updates needed
Automatic context awareness
Richer personalization
Better understanding of user needs


11. Memory in Agent Systems [02:20:00]
Memory is fundamental to building AI agents that can learn and improve over time.

Agent Architecture with Memory:

Uses context to inform decisions

Design Considerations:

Technical Terms Glossary
Context Window: The amount of text an LLM can process in a single interaction

Consolidation: The process of transferring information from working memory to long-term storage

Embeddings: Vector representations of text used for semantic search and retrieval

Episodic Memory: Memory of specific events with temporal and contextual information

MCP (Model Context Protocol): Protocol for connecting AI models with various data sources

Procedural Memory: Memory of how to perform tasks and processes

RAG (Retrieval Augmented Generation): Architecture that combines retrieval with generation

Semantic Memory: Memory of facts and general knowledge

Vector Database: Database optimized for storing and searching embeddings

Working Memory: Temporary, active memory similar to short-term memory or RAM
